Question 1medium

The word 'Ephemeral' means:

  1. Eternal
  2. Lasting for a very short time
  3. Very large
  4. Deeply meaningful
Show answer

Answer: Lasting for a very short time

'Ephemeral' means lasting for only a very short time; transitory. From Greek 'ephemeros' (lasting only a day).

Question 2medium

The antonym of 'verbose' is:

  1. Talkative
  2. Loquacious
  3. Concise
  4. Elaborate
Show answer

Answer: Concise

'Verbose' means using more words than needed. Its antonym is 'Concise' (brief and clear).

Question 3hard

The word 'Sesquipedalian' refers to:

  1. A person with a limp
  2. Long words or the habit of using them
  3. A type of poetry
  4. An ancient Roman soldier
Show answer

Answer: Long words or the habit of using them

'Sesquipedalian' (literally 'foot-and-a-half long') refers to long words or to someone who uses long, rare words.

Question 4hard

'Sycophant' means:

  1. A wise person
  2. A flatterer who seeks favour through insincere praise
  3. A person who opposes authority
  4. A philosopher
Show answer

Answer: A flatterer who seeks favour through insincere praise

A sycophant is a person who acts obsequiously towards someone important in order to gain advantage; a flatterer or 'yes-man'.

Question 5hard

In the sentence 'The ship which I booked sank', which type of clause is 'which I booked'?

  1. Adjective (relative) clause
  2. Adverbial clause
  3. Noun clause
  4. Independent clause
Show answer

Answer: Adjective (relative) clause

'Which I booked' is a relative (adjective) clause introduced by 'which'. It modifies the noun 'ship'. Relative clauses are also called adjective clauses.

Question 6hard

The word 'Ameliorate' means:

  1. To make worse
  2. To make better or improve
  3. To stay the same
  4. To destroy
Show answer

Answer: To make better or improve

'Ameliorate' means to make something bad or unsatisfactory better; to improve a situation. Antonym: 'Exacerbate' (to make worse).

Question 7hard

'Pleonasm' in language refers to:

  1. Use of metaphors
  2. Redundant use of words (saying the same thing twice)
  3. Scientific vocabulary
  4. Technical jargon
Show answer

Answer: Redundant use of words (saying the same thing twice)

Pleonasm is the use of more words than necessary to express an idea (redundancy). Examples: 'free gift', 'end result', 'past history', 'new innovation'.

Question 8easy

Which of the following is a proper noun?

  1. city
  2. river
  3. Ganges
  4. mountain
Show answer

Answer: Ganges

A proper noun names a specific person, place, or thing and is always capitalized. 'Ganges' is a proper noun (specific river). City, river, mountain are common nouns.

Question 9easy

Identify the verb in: 'She quickly ran across the field.'

  1. quickly
  2. across
  3. field
  4. ran
Show answer

Answer: ran

'Ran' is the verb (action word) in the sentence. 'Quickly' is an adverb, 'across' is a preposition, 'field' is a noun.

Question 10easy

Which word is an adjective in: 'The tall boy won the prize.'?

  1. boy
  2. won
  3. prize
  4. tall
Show answer

Answer: tall

'Tall' is an adjective that describes (modifies) the noun 'boy'. Adjectives describe the qualities of nouns.

Question 11medium

Which word is an adverb in: 'He spoke very loudly'?

  1. He
  2. spoke
  3. very
  4. loudly
Show answer

Answer: very

Both 'very' and 'loudly' are adverbs, but 'very' modifies the adverb 'loudly' (an adverb modifying another adverb). 'Loudly' modifies the verb 'spoke'. 'Very' is the best answer as it is an adverb of degree.

Question 12easy

Identify the preposition: 'The book is on the table.'

  1. book
  2. is
  3. on
  4. table
Show answer

Answer: on

'On' is a preposition showing the relationship (position) between 'book' and 'table'. Common prepositions: on, in, at, by, for, with, to, from, under, above.

Question 13easy

What is a conjunction?

  1. A word that names a person, place, or thing
  2. A word that joins words, phrases, or clauses
  3. A word that describes a noun
  4. A word that shows action
Show answer

Answer: A word that joins words, phrases, or clauses

Conjunctions join words, phrases, or clauses. Coordinating conjunctions: FANBOYS (For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So). Subordinating: because, although, when, if, since, while.

Question 14easy

Which sentence uses a pronoun?

  1. The dog barked loudly.
  2. She opened the door.
  3. The tall man arrived.
  4. Books are useful.
Show answer

Answer: She opened the door.

'She' is a pronoun replacing a noun (a person's name). Pronouns avoid repetition of nouns. Types: personal (I, you, he, she, they), relative (who, which, that), reflexive (myself, himself).

Question 15easy

An interjection is a word that:

  1. Expresses strong feeling or emotion (Oh!, Wow!, Ouch!, Hurray!)
  2. Describes a noun
  3. Shows action
  4. Connects clauses
Show answer

Answer: Expresses strong feeling or emotion (Oh!, Wow!, Ouch!, Hurray!)

Interjections express sudden feelings or emotions and are usually followed by an exclamation mark. Examples: Oh!, Wow!, Alas!, Hurray!, Ouch!, Bravo!, Oops!

Question 16medium

Which tense is used in: 'She has been studying for three hours.'?

  1. Simple past
  2. Past perfect
  3. Present perfect continuous
  4. Future continuous
Show answer

Answer: Present perfect continuous

Present perfect continuous (has/have + been + V-ing) indicates an action that started in the past and is still continuing. 'She has been studying for three hours' = started studying 3 hours ago and is still studying.

Question 17medium

Choose the correct form: 'By the time she arrived, the show ___.'

  1. had already started
  2. has already started
  3. already started
  4. will start
Show answer

Answer: had already started

Past perfect (had + past participle) is used for an action completed BEFORE another past action. 'Had already started' = the show started before she arrived. This is past perfect tense.

Question 18easy

Which sentence is in the simple future tense?

  1. She went to the market.
  2. She is going to the market.
  3. She will go to the market.
  4. She has gone to the market.
Show answer

Answer: She will go to the market.

Simple future tense: will/shall + base verb. 'She will go to the market' is simple future. 'Is going' = present continuous; 'went' = simple past; 'has gone' = present perfect.

Question 19easy

Correct the error: 'Yesterday, I am going to school.'

  1. Yesterday, I go to school.
  2. Yesterday, I went to school.
  3. Yesterday, I will go to school.
  4. Yesterday, I have gone to school.
Show answer

Answer: Yesterday, I went to school.

'Yesterday' signals simple past tense. The correct verb is 'went' (past tense of 'go'). 'Am going' is present continuous; 'will go' is future; 'have gone' is present perfect — none suits 'yesterday'.

Question 20easy

Change to passive voice: 'The teacher teaches the students.'

  1. The students were taught by the teacher.
  2. The students are taught by the teacher.
  3. The students have been taught by the teacher.
  4. The students will be taught by the teacher.
Show answer

Answer: The students are taught by the teacher.

Active: Subject + V + Object. Passive: Object + am/is/are + past participle + by + Subject. Simple present active → simple present passive: 'are taught'. 'The students are taught by the teacher.'

Question 21easy

Identify the passive voice sentence:

  1. The dog bit the boy.
  2. The boy was bitten by the dog.
  3. The boy ran fast.
  4. She sang a song.
Show answer

Answer: The boy was bitten by the dog.

Passive voice: subject receives the action. 'The boy was bitten by the dog' — 'boy' (subject) receives the action of biting. Structure: was/were + past participle (bitten).

Question 22easy

Convert to active voice: 'A letter was written by him.'

  1. He writes a letter.
  2. He wrote a letter.
  3. He is writing a letter.
  4. He has written a letter.
Show answer

Answer: He wrote a letter.

'Was written' = simple past passive → simple past active: 'He wrote a letter.' The agent (him/he) becomes the subject. Past tense 'wrote' replaces 'was written'.

Question 23easy

Convert to indirect speech: He said, 'I am happy.'

  1. He said that he is happy.
  2. He said that he was happy.
  3. He said that I am happy.
  4. He told that he was happy.
Show answer

Answer: He said that he was happy.

In indirect speech: 'said' (not 'told that') is kept; 'I' → 'he' (pronoun changes); 'am' → 'was' (tense shifts back/backshift). 'He said that he was happy.'

Question 24medium

Change to direct speech: She asked whether I was coming.

  1. She asked, 'Are you coming?'
  2. She asked, 'Was I coming?'
  3. She said, 'Are you coming?'
  4. She asked, 'Am I coming?'
Show answer

Answer: She asked, 'Are you coming?'

'Whether I was coming' → question form. 'Was' → 'are' (tense un-shifts). Pronoun 'I' (indirect, referring to person spoken to) → 'you' in direct. Uses question marks in direct speech.

Question 25easy

Fill in the blank: '___ Eiffel Tower is in Paris.'

  1. A
  2. An
  3. The
  4. No article
Show answer

Answer: The

'The' is used with unique/specific nouns: the Eiffel Tower, the Sun, the Moon, the Taj Mahal. 'A/An' are used with non-specific nouns. 'The' is the definite article.

Question 26easy

Choose the correct article: 'She is ___ honest woman.'

  1. a
  2. an
  3. the
  4. no article
Show answer

Answer: an

'An' is used before words that begin with a vowel SOUND (not just vowel letter). 'Honest' begins with a silent 'h', so the vowel sound 'o' is heard first → 'an honest woman'. 'An hour', 'an MBA'.

Question 27easy

Which sentence uses articles correctly?

  1. He is a best player.
  2. He is the best player.
  3. He is an best player.
  4. He is best player.
Show answer

Answer: He is the best player.

'The' is used with superlatives (best, tallest, most beautiful). 'He is the best player.' Superlatives use 'the' because they refer to a specific unique entity.

Question 28easy

Choose the synonym of 'Amiable':

  1. Hostile
  2. Friendly
  3. Angry
  4. Lazy
Show answer

Answer: Friendly

Amiable means friendly, pleasant, and agreeable in manner. Synonyms: friendly, affable, genial, cordial, pleasant. Antonyms: hostile, unfriendly, disagreeable.

Question 29easy

The synonym of 'Diligent' is:

  1. Lazy
  2. Hardworking
  3. Careless
  4. Weak
Show answer

Answer: Hardworking

Diligent means showing steady, earnest, and energetic effort — hardworking. Synonyms: industrious, assiduous, conscientious, persevering. Antonym: lazy, idle.

Question 30medium

Choose the synonym of 'Verbose':

  1. Brief
  2. Silent
  3. Wordy/long-winded
  4. Clear
Show answer

Answer: Wordy/long-winded

Verbose means using more words than necessary — long-winded, wordy, prolix. Antonyms: brief, concise, succinct, terse.

Question 31easy

The synonym of 'Benevolent' is:

  1. Cruel
  2. Kind/charitable
  3. Angry
  4. Miserly
Show answer

Answer: Kind/charitable

Benevolent means well-meaning and kindly, charitable. Synonyms: kind, charitable, generous, philanthropic, magnanimous. Antonyms: malevolent, cruel, selfish.

Question 32medium

Choose the synonym of 'Abhor':

  1. Love
  2. Admire
  3. Detest/hate
  4. Accept
Show answer

Answer: Detest/hate

Abhor means to regard with disgust and hatred — detest, loathe. Synonyms: hate, loathe, detest, execrate. Antonyms: love, adore, cherish.

Question 33easy

The antonym of 'Transparent' is:

  1. Clear
  2. Obvious
  3. Opaque
  4. Bright
Show answer

Answer: Opaque

Transparent means allowing light to pass through so objects can be seen clearly. Antonym: Opaque (not transparent — does not allow light through). Also antonym: translucent (partially transparent).

Question 34easy

Choose the antonym of 'Optimistic':

  1. Hopeful
  2. Positive
  3. Pessimistic
  4. Cheerful
Show answer

Answer: Pessimistic

Optimistic means hopeful and confident about the future. Antonym: Pessimistic (tending to see the worst, lacking hope). Synonyms of optimistic: hopeful, positive, sanguine.

Question 35easy

The antonym of 'Humble' is:

  1. Modest
  2. Meek
  3. Arrogant
  4. Gentle
Show answer

Answer: Arrogant

Humble means having a modest or low view of one's importance. Antonym: Arrogant (having an exaggerated sense of one's importance). Synonyms: conceited, haughty, proud.

Question 36medium

The antonym of 'Loquacious' is:

  1. Talkative
  2. Verbose
  3. Taciturn/reserved
  4. Garrulous
Show answer

Answer: Taciturn/reserved

Loquacious means tending to talk a great deal — talkative. Antonym: Taciturn (reserved in speech, saying little). Also: reticent, silent, quiet.

Question 37medium

Choose the antonym of 'Ephemeral':

  1. Temporary
  2. Fleeting
  3. Permanent/eternal
  4. Brief
Show answer

Answer: Permanent/eternal

Ephemeral means lasting for a very short time — fleeting, transient. Antonym: Permanent, eternal, everlasting, enduring.

Question 38easy

What does 'Beat around the bush' mean?

  1. To physically beat bushes
  2. To avoid coming to the main point
  3. To work hard
  4. To give up quickly
Show answer

Answer: To avoid coming to the main point

'Beat around the bush' means to avoid saying what you mean directly, to talk around the subject without addressing it directly.

Question 39easy

'Once in a blue moon' means:

  1. During full moon
  2. Every night
  3. Very rarely
  4. Every month
Show answer

Answer: Very rarely

'Once in a blue moon' means very rarely or very infrequently. A 'blue moon' is the second full moon in a calendar month (rare occurrence, hence the idiom).

Question 40easy

What does 'Let the cat out of the bag' mean?

  1. To release a cat
  2. To reveal a secret accidentally
  3. To cause confusion
  4. To be dishonest
Show answer

Answer: To reveal a secret accidentally

'Let the cat out of the bag' means to accidentally reveal a secret that was supposed to be kept hidden.

Question 41easy

'A blessing in disguise' means:

  1. A hidden curse
  2. Something that seems bad at first but turns out to be good
  3. A disguised person
  4. A small gift
Show answer

Answer: Something that seems bad at first but turns out to be good

'A blessing in disguise' describes something that initially appears negative but turns out to have a positive outcome.

Question 42easy

What does 'Bite the bullet' mean?

  1. To be violent
  2. To eat something hard
  3. To endure a difficult situation with courage
  4. To give up
Show answer

Answer: To endure a difficult situation with courage

'Bite the bullet' means to endure a painful or difficult situation bravely. Historically, soldiers bit on bullets during surgery to cope with pain before anesthesia.

Question 43easy

'Spill the beans' means:

  1. To cook beans
  2. To create a mess
  3. To reveal secret information
  4. To drop food
Show answer

Answer: To reveal secret information

'Spill the beans' means to reveal secret or confidential information, often accidentally. Similar to 'let the cat out of the bag'.

Question 44medium

A person who cannot be corrected is called:

  1. Infallible
  2. Incorrigible
  3. Invincible
  4. Incredible
Show answer

Answer: Incorrigible

Incorrigible: (of a person or their behavior) not able to be changed or reformed. 'The incorrigible child refused to follow rules.' Infallible = never makes mistakes.

Question 45easy

One who is present everywhere is called:

  1. Omniscient
  2. Omnipotent
  3. Omnivorous
  4. Omnipresent
Show answer

Answer: Omnipresent

Omnipresent: present everywhere at the same time. Omniscient: knowing everything. Omnipotent: having unlimited power. Omnivorous: eating both plants and animals.

Question 46medium

A person who hates women is called:

  1. Misogynist
  2. Misanthropist
  3. Philanthropist
  4. Feminist
Show answer

Answer: Misogynist

Misogynist: a person who dislikes, despises, or is strongly prejudiced against women. Misanthropist hates all people. Philanthropist loves humanity (gives charity).

Question 47medium

One word for 'a story in which the characters and events are symbolic':

  1. Anecdote
  2. Allegory
  3. Parable
  4. Fable
Show answer

Answer: Allegory

Allegory: a story in which characters, settings, and events represent abstract ideas or moral qualities. 'Animal Farm' is an allegory. A fable teaches a moral lesson with animals.

Question 48easy

A person who studies the stars and planets scientifically is called:

  1. Astrologer
  2. Astronomer
  3. Astrophile
  4. Cosmologist
Show answer

Answer: Astronomer

Astronomer: a scientist who studies celestial objects (stars, planets, galaxies) and phenomena. An astrologer uses celestial positions to predict human affairs (not a science).

Question 49easy

The one-word substitution for 'a person who can speak two languages' is:

  1. Monolingual
  2. Bilingual
  3. Trilingual
  4. Multilingual
Show answer

Answer: Bilingual

Bilingual: able to speak two languages fluently. Monolingual = one language. Trilingual = three languages. Multilingual/polyglot = many languages.

Question 50medium

Choose the correct sentence:

  1. Neither she nor he are wrong.
  2. Neither she nor he is wrong.
  3. Neither she nor he were wrong.
  4. Neither she nor he have been wrong.
Show answer

Answer: Neither she nor he is wrong.

With 'neither...nor', the verb agrees with the subject closer to it. 'He' (singular) is closer → singular verb 'is'. 'Neither she nor he is wrong.'

Question 51easy

Identify the error: 'She is more smarter than her brother.'

  1. She is
  2. more smarter
  3. than her brother
  4. No error
Show answer

Answer: more smarter

'More smarter' is a double comparative error. 'Smarter' already uses the comparative form (-er). Correct: 'She is smarter than her brother.' OR 'She is more smart' — but 'smarter' is preferred.

Question 52medium

Choose the correct form: 'Each of the students ___ expected to submit the assignment.'

  1. are
  2. were
  3. is
  4. have been
Show answer

Answer: is

'Each' is always singular and takes a singular verb. 'Each of the students IS expected...' Even when 'of the students' (plural) follows, 'each' determines the verb form.

Question 53easy

Fill in the blank: 'I have been living here ___ 2010.'

  1. for
  2. since
  3. during
  4. from
Show answer

Answer: since

'Since' is used with a specific point in time (2010, Monday, last year). 'For' is used with a duration (for 10 years, for a week). 'I have been living here since 2010.'

Question 54easy

'Life is a journey' is an example of:

  1. Simile
  2. Metaphor
  3. Alliteration
  4. Personification
Show answer

Answer: Metaphor

A metaphor is a direct comparison between two unlike things WITHOUT using 'like' or 'as'. 'Life IS a journey' = metaphor. 'Life is LIKE a journey' = simile.

Question 55easy

'The stars danced in the night sky' is an example of:

  1. Simile
  2. Alliteration
  3. Personification
  4. Oxymoron
Show answer

Answer: Personification

Personification attributes human qualities (dancing) to non-human things (stars). Stars don't actually dance — this human action is attributed to them.

Question 56easy

'Peter Piper picked a peck of pickled peppers' is an example of:

  1. Metaphor
  2. Alliteration (repetition of consonant sounds at the start of words)
  3. Simile
  4. Hyperbole
Show answer

Answer: Alliteration (repetition of consonant sounds at the start of words)

Alliteration: repetition of the same initial consonant sound in closely connected words. 'Peter Piper Picked a Peck of Pickled Peppers' — 'P' sound repeated.

Question 57easy

'I have told you a million times' is an example of:

  1. Simile
  2. Metaphor
  3. Hyperbole (exaggeration for emphasis)
  4. Irony
Show answer

Answer: Hyperbole (exaggeration for emphasis)

Hyperbole is deliberate exaggeration for emphasis or effect. 'A million times' = exaggeration (they didn't literally say it a million times). Other examples: 'I'm so hungry I could eat a horse.'

Question 58medium

Which sentence uses a conditional Type 1 (real/possible condition)?

  1. If I were rich, I would travel the world.
  2. If I had studied, I would have passed.
  3. If it rains, we will cancel the trip.
  4. If water freezes, it becomes ice.
Show answer

Answer: If it rains, we will cancel the trip.

Type 1 conditional: if + present simple, will + base verb. It expresses real/possible future conditions. 'If it rains, we will cancel the trip.' Type 0 = general truth; Type 2 = unreal present; Type 3 = unreal past.

Question 59medium

Which sentence uses a conditional Type 2 (unreal/hypothetical present)?

  1. If you heat water to 100°C, it boils.
  2. If I were the president, I would reform education.
  3. If she studies hard, she will pass.
  4. If they had left early, they would have caught the train.
Show answer

Answer: If I were the president, I would reform education.

Type 2 conditional: if + past simple, would + base verb. Expresses hypothetical or unreal present/future situations. 'If I were the president' — impossible or very unlikely in the present.

Question 60medium

Which sentence uses a conditional Type 3 (unreal past)?

  1. If she were here, she would help.
  2. If you leave now, you will arrive on time.
  3. If they had left early, they would have caught the train.
  4. If I heat metal, it expands.
Show answer

Answer: If they had left early, they would have caught the train.

Type 3 conditional: if + past perfect, would have + past participle. Refers to unreal/imagined past situations and their hypothetical results. 'If they had left early, they would have caught the train.'

Question 61easy

Which modal verb expresses strong obligation or necessity?

  1. Can
  2. May
  3. Should
  4. Must
Show answer

Answer: Must

'Must' expresses strong obligation or necessity (internal compulsion). 'Should' expresses advice or mild obligation. 'Can' = ability. 'May' = permission or possibility. E.g., 'You must wear a seatbelt.'

Question 62medium

Choose the sentence with correct use of 'can' vs 'may':

  1. Can I borrow your pen? (polite request — formal)
  2. May I borrow your pen? (polite request/permission — correct formal)
  3. May she swim very fast. (ability)
  4. He can perhaps come tomorrow. (permission)
Show answer

Answer: May I borrow your pen? (polite request/permission — correct formal)

'May' is used for polite requests/permission in formal contexts: 'May I borrow your pen?' 'Can' expresses ability (she can swim fast) or informal permission. In formal usage, 'may' is preferred for requesting permission.

Question 63medium

Which sentence uses 'would' correctly to express a past habit?

  1. He would go to the park every evening when he was young.
  2. Would you like some tea?
  3. He would have done it if asked.
  4. I would prefer coffee.
Show answer

Answer: He would go to the park every evening when he was young.

'Would' is used to describe repeated past habits: 'He would go to the park every evening when he was young.' This usage is similar to 'used to' but 'would' is only for actions, not states.

Question 64medium

Which sentence contains a defining (restrictive) relative clause?

  1. My sister, who lives in Chennai, is a doctor.
  2. The book that I borrowed from the library was fascinating.
  3. The Taj Mahal, which is in Agra, is a UNESCO site.
  4. Mount Everest, which is the highest peak, is in Nepal.
Show answer

Answer: The book that I borrowed from the library was fascinating.

A defining/restrictive relative clause identifies which specific person/thing is meant — no commas used. 'The book that I borrowed' tells us which specific book. Non-defining clauses (with commas) add extra information about an already-identified noun.

Question 65medium

Which sentence uses a non-defining (non-restrictive) relative clause?

  1. The student who passed the exam got a medal.
  2. The girl that called you is waiting outside.
  3. My father, who is a doctor, lives in Madurai.
  4. The car that she bought is red.
Show answer

Answer: My father, who is a doctor, lives in Madurai.

A non-defining relative clause (set off by commas) adds extra information about a noun already specifically identified. 'My father' is already identified, and 'who is a doctor' is additional, non-essential information.

Question 66easy

Choose the sentence where a gerund is correctly used as the subject:

  1. He enjoys to swim every morning.
  2. Swimming is good exercise.
  3. She wants swimming in the lake.
  4. To swims is her hobby.
Show answer

Answer: Swimming is good exercise.

A gerund (verb + -ing) used as the subject of a sentence: 'Swimming is good exercise.' Gerunds can function as subjects, objects, and complements. After 'enjoy', gerund is correct: 'He enjoys swimming.'

Question 67medium

Which verb is followed by a gerund (not an infinitive)?

  1. want
  2. decide
  3. enjoy
  4. hope
Show answer

Answer: enjoy

'Enjoy' is always followed by a gerund: 'I enjoy reading.' Verbs followed by gerund: enjoy, avoid, suggest, consider, keep, mind, finish, practice. Verbs followed by infinitive: want, decide, hope, plan, agree, refuse.

Question 68easy

Which verb is followed by an infinitive?

  1. avoid
  2. suggest
  3. decide
  4. finish
Show answer

Answer: decide

'Decide' is followed by an infinitive: 'She decided to leave early.' Verbs followed by infinitive: decide, agree, want, plan, hope, refuse, promise, learn, manage. Some verbs take both (like 'start', 'begin', 'continue').

Question 69hard

Identify the error in this sentence: 'Neither the students nor the teacher were present.'

  1. 'Neither' should be 'Either'
  2. 'were' should be 'was' (verb agrees with the subject closer to it — 'teacher')
  3. 'nor' should be 'or'
  4. No error
Show answer

Answer: 'were' should be 'was' (verb agrees with the subject closer to it — 'teacher')

With 'neither...nor' (and 'either...or'), the verb agrees with the subject nearest to it (proximate agreement rule). 'Teacher' (singular) is nearest, so the verb should be 'was': 'Neither the students nor the teacher was present.'

Question 70medium

Identify the error: 'Each of the boys have submitted their assignment.'

  1. 'boys' should be 'boy'
  2. 'their' should be 'his'
  3. 'have' should be 'has' — 'each' takes singular verb
  4. No error
Show answer

Answer: 'have' should be 'has' — 'each' takes singular verb

'Each' is always singular and takes a singular verb: 'Each of the boys has submitted.' Similarly: 'everyone has', 'everybody is', 'each one of them was'. Distributive pronouns/adjectives (each, every, either, neither) take singular verbs.

Question 71easy

Identify the error: 'The news are very disturbing today.'

  1. 'are' should be 'is' — 'news' is uncountable and always singular
  2. 'very' should be 'much'
  3. 'today' should be 'currently'
  4. No error
Show answer

Answer: 'are' should be 'is' — 'news' is uncountable and always singular

'News' is an uncountable (mass) noun in English and always takes a singular verb: 'The news is very disturbing today.' Other such nouns: information, advice, furniture, luggage, equipment, knowledge, progress.

Question 72easy

Choose the correctly punctuated sentence:

  1. However I was not surprised.
  2. However, I was not surprised.
  3. However; I was not surprised.
  4. However: I was not surprised.
Show answer

Answer: However, I was not surprised.

Conjunctive adverbs (however, therefore, moreover, nevertheless, consequently, furthermore) that begin a sentence or clause are followed by a comma: 'However, I was not surprised.' A semicolon is used before them when joining two independent clauses: 'It rained; however, we continued.'

Question 73medium

Which sentence demonstrates correct use of a semicolon?

  1. She loves reading; and she visits the library weekly.
  2. It was a long day; I was exhausted.
  3. He is tall; but she is short.
  4. We played cricket; despite the rain.
Show answer

Answer: It was a long day; I was exhausted.

Semicolons join two related independent clauses without a coordinating conjunction: 'It was a long day; I was exhausted.' Do NOT use a semicolon before coordinating conjunctions (and, but, or) — use a comma instead.

Question 74medium

The word 'benign' means:

  1. Dangerous and threatening
  2. Gentle, kind, or not harmful (especially of a tumour)
  3. Powerful and dominant
  4. Confused and uncertain
Show answer

Answer: Gentle, kind, or not harmful (especially of a tumour)

Benign: gentle, kind, or harmless. In medicine, a benign tumour is non-cancerous and not harmful (opposite: malignant). Synonyms: harmless, mild, gentle, favorable. The antonym is 'malignant' or 'hostile'.

Question 75easy

The word 'frugal' means:

  1. Generous and lavish
  2. Careful with money and resources; economical
  3. Energetic and enthusiastic
  4. Confused and indecisive
Show answer

Answer: Careful with money and resources; economical

Frugal: sparing or economical with money and food. A frugal person avoids waste. Synonyms: thrifty, economical, careful, prudent. Antonyms: extravagant, wasteful, lavish, spendthrift.

Question 76easy

The word 'lucid' means:

  1. Dark and confusing
  2. Clearly expressed and easy to understand
  3. Angry and aggressive
  4. Mysterious and secretive
Show answer

Answer: Clearly expressed and easy to understand

Lucid: expressed clearly; easy to understand; or mentally clear (lucid explanation, lucid dream). From Latin 'lucidus' = light/clear. Synonyms: clear, coherent, intelligible. Antonyms: confusing, muddled, obscure.

Question 77medium

The word 'tenacious' means:

  1. Fragile and delicate
  2. Holding firmly to something; persistent
  3. Temporary and fleeting
  4. Generous and giving

Answer: Holding firmly to something; persistent

Tenacious: holding firmly to something; not giving up easily; persistent. 'Tenacious grip', 'tenacious in pursuit of goals'. From Latin 'tenax' = holding fast. Synonyms: persistent, determined, stubborn, resolute.

Question 78easy

What is the one-word substitute for 'a word or phrase that reads the same forwards and backwards'?

  1. Palindrome
  2. Oxymoron
  3. Anagram
  4. Homophone
Show answer

Answer: Palindrome

Palindrome: a word, phrase, or sequence that reads the same forwards and backwards. Examples: 'radar', 'level', 'madam', 'civic', 'Malayalam' (a palindrome in a language!), 'A man a plan a canal Panama'.

Question 79easy

What is the one-word substitute for 'a word that has the same sound as another but different meaning and spelling'?

  1. Synonym
  2. Antonym
  3. Homophone
  4. Homograph
Show answer

Answer: Homophone

Homophone: words that sound the same but have different meanings and spellings. Examples: hear/here, their/there/they're, write/right, flour/flower, mail/male, knight/night. Homograph: same spelling, different meaning (e.g., 'bank').

Question 80easy

What is the one-word substitute for 'one who studies the stars and celestial bodies scientifically'?

  1. Astrologer
  2. Astronomer
  3. Astrophysicist
  4. Cosmologist
Show answer

Answer: Astronomer

Astronomer: a scientist who studies stars, planets, and other celestial bodies. Astrologer: one who studies astrology (belief that stars influence human affairs). Astrophysicist: studies physical properties of celestial bodies. Cosmologist: studies the origin and evolution of the universe.

Question 81easy

What is the one-word substitute for 'a government by the people'?

  1. Monarchy
  2. Aristocracy
  3. Democracy
  4. Theocracy
Show answer

Answer: Democracy

Democracy: government by the people, for the people (from Greek 'demos' = people + 'kratos' = power). Monarchy = rule by a king/queen. Aristocracy = rule by nobility. Theocracy = rule by religious leaders. Autocracy = rule by one person with absolute power.

Question 82easy

What is the one-word substitute for 'one who eats both plants and animals'?

  1. Herbivore
  2. Carnivore
  3. Omnivore
  4. Insectivore
Show answer

Answer: Omnivore

Omnivore: an organism that eats both plants and animals. Herbivore: eats only plants (cow, deer). Carnivore: eats only meat (tiger, lion). Insectivore: eats insects. Humans are omnivores.

Question 83easy

Identify the figure of speech in: 'The wind whispered through the trees.'

  1. Simile
  2. Metaphor
  3. Personification
  4. Hyperbole
Show answer

Answer: Personification

Personification: giving human qualities or actions to non-human things. Wind cannot literally 'whisper' — this is a human action. More examples: 'The sun smiled down on us', 'The flowers danced in the breeze', 'Time waits for no man'.

Question 84easy

Identify the figure of speech in: 'He ran as fast as a cheetah.'

  1. Metaphor
  2. Simile
  3. Alliteration
  4. Oxymoron
Show answer

Answer: Simile

Simile: a comparison using 'as' or 'like'. 'He ran as fast as a cheetah.' Metaphor makes a direct comparison without 'like' or 'as': 'He is a cheetah.' Key marker words for simile: as...as, like, resembles, similar to.

Question 85easy

Identify the figure of speech in: 'She is the black sheep of the family.'

  1. Simile
  2. Alliteration
  3. Metaphor
  4. Personification
Show answer

Answer: Metaphor

Metaphor: a direct comparison without 'like' or 'as'. 'She is the black sheep' — does not literally mean she is a sheep; it means she is the odd one out or disgrace of the family. More metaphors: 'Life is a journey', 'Time is money'.

Question 86easy

Identify the figure of speech in: 'Peter Piper picked a peck of pickled peppers.'

  1. Assonance
  2. Alliteration
  3. Onomatopoeia
  4. Anaphora
Show answer

Answer: Alliteration

Alliteration: repetition of the same consonant sound at the beginning of closely connected words. 'Peter Piper picked a peck of pickled peppers' — P sound repeated. Other examples: 'She sells seashells by the seashore', 'Big black bugs bit big black bears'.

Question 87easy

The antonym of 'indigenous' is:

  1. Native
  2. Local
  3. Foreign/Alien
  4. Endemic
Show answer

Answer: Foreign/Alien

Indigenous means originating or occurring naturally in a place; native. Its antonym is 'foreign' or 'alien' (from another place). Synonyms of indigenous: native, aboriginal, endemic. E.g., Indigenous plants of India vs. introduced/exotic species.

Question 88medium

Choose the sentence where 'fewer' and 'less' are used correctly:

  1. There are less students in class today.
  2. I have fewer money than she does.
  3. There are fewer students and less noise in the library today.
  4. He has less books than me.
Show answer

Answer: There are fewer students and less noise in the library today.

'Fewer' is for countable nouns (fewer students, fewer cars, fewer days). 'Less' is for uncountable nouns (less noise, less water, less time, less money). Common error: 'less people' should be 'fewer people'.

Question 89easy

Which sentence is in the passive voice?

  1. The chef cooked a delicious meal.
  2. A delicious meal was cooked by the chef.
  3. The chef has cooked dinner.
  4. The chef is cooking dinner.
Show answer

Answer: A delicious meal was cooked by the chef.

Passive voice: subject + 'to be' + past participle (+ 'by' + agent). 'A delicious meal was cooked by the chef.' The object of the active sentence becomes the subject in the passive. Active: 'The chef cooked a meal.' → Passive: 'A meal was cooked by the chef.'

Question 90medium

Change to passive voice: 'The government has announced new policies.'

  1. New policies have been announced by the government.
  2. New policies was announced by the government.
  3. New policies are announced by the government.
  4. New policies had been announced by the government.
Show answer

Answer: New policies have been announced by the government.

Present perfect active: have/has + past participle → Present perfect passive: have/has + been + past participle. 'The government has announced' → 'New policies have been announced by the government.' The subject of the passive is 'policies' (plural → 'have been').

Question 91medium

Change to indirect speech: He said, 'I will come tomorrow.'

  1. He said that he will come tomorrow.
  2. He said that he would come the next day.
  3. He told that he came the next day.
  4. He said that he came yesterday.
Show answer

Answer: He said that he would come the next day.

Reporting verb in past tense → tense shift: 'will' → 'would'; 'tomorrow' → 'the next day'. Also: 'today' → 'that day', 'yesterday' → 'the day before', 'here' → 'there', 'come' → 'go'. Pronoun 'I' → 'he'.

Question 92medium

Change to direct speech: She asked me where I lived.

  1. She said, 'Where did you live?'
  2. She asked, 'Where do you live?'
  3. She asked, 'Where did I live?'
  4. She told me, 'Where you live?'
Show answer

Answer: She asked, 'Where do you live?'

Converting indirect question to direct: remove 'that'/'whether'; change tense back ('lived' → 'live'); change pronouns; use question word order and add '?'. 'She asked where I lived' → 'She asked, "Where do you live?"'

Question 93easy

Which is the correct spelling?

  1. Accomodation
  2. Acomodation
  3. Accommodation
  4. Acommodation
Show answer

Answer: Accommodation

Correct spelling: 'Accommodation' (2 c's + 2 m's). A common spelling error. Memory trick: 'accommodate has enough room for 2 c's and 2 m's'. Other commonly misspelled words: occurrence (2 c's + 2 r's), necessary (1 c + 2 s's), separate (not 'seperate').

Question 94medium

Choose the sentence that correctly uses the apostrophe:

  1. The book's on the table are mine.
  2. The childrens' toys are scattered.
  3. The children's toys are scattered.
  4. The childrens toys are scattered.
Show answer

Answer: The children's toys are scattered.

For irregular plurals not ending in 's' (children, men, women, mice, geese), add apostrophe + s: children's, men's, women's. For regular plurals ending in 's', add only apostrophe: students' books, teachers' lounge.

Question 95easy

What does the idiom 'to add fuel to the fire' mean?

  1. To help solve a problem
  2. To make a bad situation worse
  3. To start a new project
  4. To calm someone down
Show answer

Answer: To make a bad situation worse

'To add fuel to the fire' means to make an already bad situation worse. Similar idioms: 'pour oil on troubled waters' (OPPOSITE — to calm a situation), 'fan the flames' (also means to worsen a situation), 'rub salt in the wound' (make things worse).

Question 96easy

What does the idiom 'to burn the midnight oil' mean?

  1. To waste energy
  2. To work or study late into the night
  3. To celebrate a festival
  4. To cause a fire hazard
Show answer

Answer: To work or study late into the night

'To burn the midnight oil' means to work or study very late at night. Origin: before electricity, people used oil lamps; working late meant burning more oil. Similar: 'burn the candle at both ends' (overwork), 'pull an all-nighter'.

Answer: To say or do exactly the right thing

'To hit the nail on the head' means to describe something exactly correctly or to do exactly the right thing. Similar expressions: 'spot on', 'bull's eye', 'right on the mark', 'on the money'.

Question 98medium

  1. A very severe storm
  2. A great fuss or upset over something trivial
  3. Drinking too much tea
  4. A small incident that causes damage
Show answer

Answer: A great fuss or upset over something trivial

'A storm in a teacup' (British)/tempest in a teapot (American) means making a big fuss about a small/trivial matter. The dramatic scale of a 'storm' contrasts with the tiny size of a 'teacup'. Similar: 'mountain out of a molehill'.

'All that glitters is not gold' (Shakespeare's Merchant of Venice) means appearances can be deceptive; not everything that looks valuable or attractive actually is. Similar: 'Don't judge a book by its cover', 'Beauty is only skin deep'.

Question 100easy

  2. Fixing a problem promptly prevents it from becoming worse
  3. Always count before acting
  4. Time management is the most important skill
Show answer

Answer: Fixing a problem promptly prevents it from becoming worse

'A stitch in time saves nine' means that timely action to deal with a small problem prevents it from growing into a much bigger one (one stitch now saves having to make nine later). Similar: 'Prevention is better than cure'.

'Don't count your chickens before they hatch' means don't make plans based on something that hasn't happened yet. Don't assume success before it's certain. Similar: 'Don't cross your bridges before you come to them'.

Question 103hard

Question 104easy

In a passage, the 'main idea' is:

  1. The first sentence of the passage
  2. The last sentence of the passage
  3. The central thought that the passage is primarily about
  4. A minor supporting detail
Show answer

Answer: The central thought that the passage is primarily about

The main idea (central idea/thesis) is the primary message or point the author wants to convey. It may be stated directly (topic sentence) or implied. Supporting details, examples, and evidence all reinforce the main idea.

Question 105easy

An 'inference' in reading comprehension means:

  1. Information directly stated in the text
  2. A conclusion drawn from evidence and reasoning, not directly stated
  3. A quote from the author
  4. A definition given in the text
Show answer

Answer: A conclusion drawn from evidence and reasoning, not directly stated

An inference is a conclusion drawn using evidence from the text plus your own reasoning — it is not directly stated. Example: If a passage says 'He wore a heavy coat and shivered', you can infer it was cold. Reading between the lines.

Question 106medium

'Tone' of a passage refers to:

  1. The volume of the writing
  2. The author's attitude or feeling toward the subject
  3. The speed at which the passage should be read
  4. The vocabulary level of the passage
Show answer

Answer: The author's attitude or feeling toward the subject

Tone: the author's attitude toward the subject or audience, expressed through word choice, style, and perspective. Tones can be: formal, informal, humorous, serious, sarcastic, sympathetic, critical, optimistic, pessimistic, objective, subjective.

Question 107hard

Choose the correct sentence (error spotting):

  1. He is one of those boys who plays cricket every day.
  2. He is one of those boys who play cricket every day.
  3. He is one of those boy who play cricket every day.
  4. He is one of those boys who were playing cricket every day.
Show answer

Answer: He is one of those boys who play cricket every day.

'One of those boys who' — the relative clause modifies 'boys' (plural), so the verb must be plural: 'who play'. Many learners incorrectly use 'plays' (assuming the antecedent is 'one'). The structure 'one of those + noun + who + plural verb' is correct.

Sanguine: optimistic or positive, especially in a difficult situation. From Latin 'sanguis' = blood (blood was associated with a warm, hopeful temperament). Synonyms: optimistic, hopeful, positive, buoyant. Antonyms: pessimistic, gloomy, despondent.

Pedantic: giving undue attention to formal rules, minor details, or book learning. A pedantic person is overly concerned with precision and correctness. Synonyms: nitpicking, fussy, hair-splitting. Often used negatively.

Cynical: believing that people are generally selfish and dishonest; distrustful of human sincerity. Synonyms: skeptical, distrustful, suspicious, pessimistic. Antonyms: trusting, optimistic, idealistic. From the ancient Greek Cynics who questioned societal values.

Question 114hard

Select the sentence with the correct use of 'lay' vs 'lie':

  1. I need to lay down for a while.
  2. Yesterday, he laid the book on the table.
  3. The cat was lieing on the sofa.
  4. She laid on the grass and watched the clouds.
Show answer

Answer: Yesterday, he laid the book on the table.

'Lay' (transitive) needs an object: lay/laid/laid/laying. 'Lie' (intransitive) = to recline: lie/lay/lain/lying. 'He laid the book on the table' = correct (laid = past of lay, transitive). 'I need to lie down' is correct (not 'lay down'). 'She lay on the grass' (past of lie intransitive).

Question 115medium

Choose the sentence with the correct use of 'raise' vs 'rise':

  1. The sun will rise tomorrow morning.
  2. The sun will raise tomorrow morning.
  3. Please rise your hand if you agree.
  4. The price of petrol raised suddenly.
Show answer

Answer: The sun will rise tomorrow morning.

'Rise' (intransitive — no object): rise/rose/risen. 'Raise' (transitive — needs an object): raise/raised/raised. 'The sun rises' (no object). 'Please raise your hand' (hand = object). 'Prices rose' (intransitive). 'The government raised prices' (transitive).

Autocracy: a system of government where one person (autocrat) holds unlimited power. Synonyms: dictatorship, despotism, tyranny. Oligarchy = rule by a small group; Anarchy = absence of government; Theocracy = rule by religious leaders; Democracy = people's rule.

Question 118medium

  2. Theist
  3. Agnostic
  4. Deist
Show answer

Answer: Agnostic

Agnostic: a person who believes that the existence of God is unknown or unknowable. Atheist: believes God does not exist. Theist: believes in God. Deist: believes in God based on reason/nature, not religious revelation. Agnostic (Greek): 'without knowledge'.

Question 119medium

  2. Monologue
  3. Soliloquy
  4. Eulogy

Answer: Soliloquy

Soliloquy: a literary device where a character speaks their thoughts aloud to themselves, especially in a play. Famous example: Hamlet's 'To be or not to be' soliloquy. Monologue = long speech by one person to an audience. Dialogue = conversation between two or more people.

Cartography: the art or science of drawing maps. A cartographer makes maps. Etymology: French 'carte' (map) + Greek 'graphia' (writing/drawing). Topography: study of landforms; Geography: study of the earth and its features.

Allegory: a narrative where characters, events, and settings represent abstract ideas or moral qualities. Famous allegories: Animal Farm (George Orwell — political allegory), The Pilgrim's Progress (John Bunyan — Christian allegory), Plato's Allegory of the Cave.

Question 129easy

Exclamatory sentences express strong emotion and end with an exclamation mark. They begin with 'What' or 'How': 'What a beautiful sunset!', 'How wonderful!'. Declarative = statement; Interrogative = question; Imperative = command or request.

Question 130easy

Identify the type of sentence: 'Please submit your assignment by Friday.'

  1. Declarative
  2. Interrogative
  3. Imperative
  4. Exclamatory
Show answer

Answer: Imperative

Imperative sentences give a command, request, advice, or instruction. They often begin with a verb in base form and the subject 'you' is implied: 'Please submit', 'Close the door', 'Be careful'. They can end with a period or exclamation mark.

Question 131medium

A compound sentence consists of:

  1. One independent clause only
  2. Two or more independent clauses joined by a coordinating conjunction
  3. One independent clause and one dependent clause
  4. Only dependent clauses
Show answer

Answer: Two or more independent clauses joined by a coordinating conjunction

Compound sentence: two or more independent clauses joined by coordinating conjunctions (FANBOYS: For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So) or a semicolon. Example: 'She studied hard, and she passed the exam.' Each clause can stand alone.

Question 132medium

A complex sentence consists of:

  1. Two or more independent clauses
  2. One independent clause and at least one dependent clause
  3. Only dependent clauses
  4. Two simple sentences
Show answer

Answer: One independent clause and at least one dependent clause

Complex sentence: one independent clause + one or more dependent (subordinate) clauses, joined by subordinating conjunctions (because, although, when, since, if, unless, after, before, while, etc.). Example: 'Although it was raining, she went for a walk.'

Question 133hard

Choose the sentence that contains a dangling modifier:

  1. Walking to school, Ravi saw an accident.
  2. Walking to school, an accident was seen.
  3. While walking to school, Ravi noticed an accident.
  4. Ravi, while walking to school, noticed an accident.
Show answer

Answer: Walking to school, an accident was seen.

Dangling modifier: a modifying phrase that doesn't clearly refer to any specific word in the sentence. 'Walking to school, an accident was seen.' — who was walking? Not the accident! It should be: 'Walking to school, Ravi saw an accident.' (the doer of 'walking' must follow immediately).

Question 134easy

What does 'RSVP' stand for in formal invitations?

  1. Respond Soon, Very Please
  2. Répondez s'il vous plaît (French — 'Please respond')
  3. Reserve Seats Via Phone
  4. Request for Special Venue Preparation
Show answer

Answer: Répondez s'il vous plaît (French — 'Please respond')

RSVP = French 'Répondez s'il vous plaît' meaning 'Please respond' or 'Please reply'. Used in formal invitations (parties, weddings, events) to request a confirmation of attendance. A common French phrase in English usage.

Question 135medium

Which sentence is written in the future perfect tense?

  1. She will write the report tomorrow.
  2. She is writing the report.
  3. She will have written the report by tomorrow.
  4. She had written the report.
Show answer

Answer: She will have written the report by tomorrow.

Future perfect: will have + past participle. Expresses an action that will be completed before a specific future time: 'She will have written the report by tomorrow.' Future simple: will + base verb. Past perfect: had + past participle.

Question 136easy

Which sentence is written in the past perfect tense?

  1. She was writing when he called.
  2. She wrote the report yesterday.
  3. She had already written the report when he called.
  4. She will write the report soon.
Show answer

Answer: She had already written the report when he called.

Past perfect: had + past participle. Expresses an action completed before another past action: 'She had already written the report when he called.' The past perfect action (had written) happened before the simple past action (called).

Question 137medium

The figure of speech 'understatement' means:

  1. Exaggerating something to extreme
  2. Saying something is less significant or impressive than it actually is
  3. Using comparisons with 'like' or 'as'
  4. Using the same consonant sounds repeatedly
Show answer

Answer: Saying something is less significant or impressive than it actually is

Understatement: presenting something as less significant than it is. Examples: 'It's just a scratch' (for a serious wound), 'Not bad' (for something excellent). Opposite of hyperbole. Litotes (a form of understatement) uses double negatives: 'not unhappy' = quite happy.

Question 138easy

Choose the word with the correct prefix to mean 'not possible':

  1. Unpossible
  2. Impossibly
  3. Impossible
  4. Dispossible
Show answer

Answer: Impossible

'Impossible' = 'im-' + 'possible'. The prefix 'im-' (variant of 'in-') means 'not' and is used before words starting with 'p', 'b', 'm': impossible, impolite, imbalanced, immature. 'in-' is used in other cases: incorrect, invisible, inactive.

Question 139easy

Which of the following is an example of onomatopoeia?

  1. The soldier was brave as a lion.
  2. The bee buzzed past my ear.
  3. Life is a journey with many crossroads.
  4. Every cloud has a silver lining.
Show answer

Answer: The bee buzzed past my ear.

Onomatopoeia: words that phonetically imitate the sound they describe. 'Buzz' sounds like the sound a bee makes. Other examples: hiss, sizzle, crackle, murmur, clang, splash, crash, meow, cuckoo, drip, thud.

Question 140easy

Select the correct sentence:

  1. He has wrote a new novel.
  2. He have written a new novel.
  3. He has written a new novel.
  4. He is wrote a new novel.
Show answer

Answer: He has written a new novel.

Present perfect: have/has + past participle. 'Write' → past participle is 'written' (irregular verb). 'He has written a new novel.' Common errors: 'has wrote' (wrong — 'wrote' is simple past, not past participle) or 'have written' with third person singular (should be 'has').

Question 141hard

The literary term 'catharsis' refers to:

  1. The introduction to a story
  2. The emotional purification or release felt by the audience through art, especially tragedy
  3. The climax of a drama
  4. The final resolution of a plot
Show answer

Answer: The emotional purification or release felt by the audience through art, especially tragedy

Catharsis (Greek: 'cleansing'): Aristotle's concept that tragedy purges/releases pent-up emotions (especially pity and fear) in the audience, leaving them feeling emotionally cleansed and lighter. Core concept in classical Greek drama.

Question 142hard

The use of 'apostrophe' as a literary device means:

  1. The punctuation mark (')
  2. A figure of speech where an absent person, abstract idea, or nonhuman entity is directly addressed
  3. A footnote reference
  4. A historical reference in a text
Show answer

Answer: A figure of speech where an absent person, abstract idea, or nonhuman entity is directly addressed

Apostrophe (literary device): directly addressing an absent person, abstract concept, or nonhuman thing as if it were present. Examples: 'O Death, where is your sting?' (addressing Death), 'Twinkle, twinkle, little star, how I wonder what you are' (addressing a star).

Question 143medium

Choose the sentence with the correct use of articles:

  1. She is an honest woman.
  2. She is a honest woman.
  3. She is the honest woman.
  4. She is honest a woman.
Show answer

Answer: She is an honest woman.

Use 'an' before words beginning with a vowel sound, regardless of spelling. 'Honest' begins with a silent 'h', making the vowel sound 'o': so 'an honest'. Similarly: an hour, an heir, an honor. But: a hero (h is pronounced), a hospital (h pronounced), a university (sounds like 'yoo').

Question 144hard

Which sentence contains a split infinitive?

  1. She decided to leave quickly.
  2. She wanted to boldly go where no one had gone before.
  3. She hoped to finish her work before dinner.
  4. To err is human; to forgive, divine.
Show answer

Answer: She wanted to boldly go where no one had gone before.

Split infinitive: an adverb or other word placed between 'to' and the base verb. 'To boldly go' — 'boldly' splits 'to go'. Traditionally considered incorrect but now widely accepted. 'To really understand' is a split infinitive. The most famous example: 'To boldly go' from Star Trek.

Question 145medium

'Nemesis' means:

  1. A close friend
  2. An inescapable agent of punishment or downfall; a long-standing rival
  3. A type of heroic character
  4. A happy coincidence
Show answer

Answer: An inescapable agent of punishment or downfall; a long-standing rival

Nemesis: a person's inescapable punishment, or a long-standing rival/opponent. Originally, Nemesis was the Greek goddess of retributive justice. In modern use: 'Batman's nemesis is the Joker', 'Overconfidence was his nemesis.' Related: 'poetic justice'.

Question 146easy

Choose the sentence with the correct preposition:

  1. She is married with a doctor.
  2. She is married to a doctor.
  3. She is married by a doctor.
  4. She is married for a doctor.
Show answer

Answer: She is married to a doctor.

'Married to' is the correct preposition: 'She is married to a doctor.' Common preposition errors: married to (not married with/by/for). 'Married by' = the officiant who performed the marriage. 'She was married by the priest' vs. 'She is married to John'.

Question 147medium

The synonym of 'innocuous' is:

  1. Dangerous
  2. Harmless
  3. Confusing
  4. Unusual
Show answer

Answer: Harmless

Innocuous: not harmful or offensive; completely harmless. From Latin 'in-' (not) + 'nocuus' (harmful). Synonyms: harmless, benign, inoffensive, safe, innocent. Antonyms: harmful, dangerous, offensive, toxic. 'An innocuous remark' does no damage.

Question 148medium

The synonym of 'ostentatious' is:

  1. Modest and restrained
  2. Showy and designed to impress
  3. Simple and elegant
  4. Dull and unattractive
Show answer

Answer: Showy and designed to impress

Ostentatious: characterized by displaying wealth or knowledge in a way that is meant to impress others; showy. Synonyms: showy, pretentious, flamboyant, flashy, gaudy. Antonyms: modest, humble, understated, restrained.

Question 149medium

The synonym of 'insolent' is:

  1. Respectful and polite
  2. Boldly rude or disrespectful; impudent
  3. Shy and timid
  4. Generous and kind
Show answer

Answer: Boldly rude or disrespectful; impudent

Insolent: showing a rude and arrogant lack of respect; impudent. Synonyms: rude, impudent, disrespectful, arrogant, brazen, cheeky. Antonyms: respectful, polite, humble, deferential.

Question 150medium

Arrange these sentences to make a coherent paragraph: (1) First, gather all your materials. (2) Finally, review your completed essay. (3) Then, create an outline. (4) Start by choosing your topic.

  1. 1-3-2-4
  2. 4-1-3-2
  3. 4-3-1-2
  4. 1-4-3-2
Show answer

Answer: 4-1-3-2

Correct sequence for essay writing: 4 (Choose topic) → 1 (Gather materials) → 3 (Create outline) → 2 (Review completed essay). The logical order uses transitional words: 'Start by...' (first step), 'First...' (second), 'Then...' (third), 'Finally...' (last step).

Question 151medium

What does the phrase 'in lieu of' mean?

  1. In addition to
  2. Instead of
  3. Because of
  4. In front of
Show answer

Answer: Instead of

'In lieu of' means 'instead of' or 'in place of'. From French 'lieu' = place. 'He accepted cash in lieu of a gift card.' Common in formal and business language. Synonyms: instead of, in place of, as a substitute for.

Question 152easy

Which of the following is a correct example of a 'simile'?

  1. Her smile was the sunshine of his day.
  2. He was as brave as a lion in battle.
  3. The forest whispered secrets to the wanderers.
  4. His words were daggers to her heart.
Show answer

Answer: He was as brave as a lion in battle.

Simile: a comparison using 'like' or 'as'. 'He was as brave as a lion' uses 'as...as' to compare. The others are metaphors (direct comparisons without 'like'/'as') or personification. Key identifier: 'like' or 'as' is explicitly present.

Question 153hard

Which sentence demonstrates 'litotes' (understatement using negation)?

  1. I'm so hungry I could eat a horse.
  2. He's not the brightest student in the class.
  3. She ran like the wind.
  4. He was a roaring lion in the courtroom.
Show answer

Answer: He's not the brightest student in the class.

Litotes: a form of understatement using a negative to affirm the positive. 'Not the brightest' = fairly dull (saying the same thing in a more indirect, understated way). Other examples: 'Not bad' = good; 'Not unpopular' = quite popular; 'Not a small amount' = a large amount.

Question 154medium

An 'epigram' is:

  1. A long narrative poem
  2. A short witty saying or poem expressing a clever idea
  3. A list of references in a book
  4. The dedication at the beginning of a book
Show answer

Answer: A short witty saying or poem expressing a clever idea

Epigram: a pithy, witty saying or short poem with a clever twist. Examples: 'I can resist everything except temptation' (Oscar Wilde), 'I have nothing to declare except my genius' (Oscar Wilde). Distinct from epigraph (quote at the start of a book) and epitaph (inscription on a tombstone).

Question 155medium

What is a 'portmanteau word'?

  1. A French word used in English
  2. A word formed by combining parts of two or more words
  3. A very long compound word
  4. A word borrowed from Latin
Show answer

Answer: A word formed by combining parts of two or more words

Portmanteau word: a word created by blending the sounds and meanings of two words. Examples: brunch (breakfast + lunch), smog (smoke + fog), motel (motor + hotel), blog (web + log), emoticon (emotion + icon), Bollywood (Bombay + Hollywood), pixel (picture + element).

Question 156easy

'Onomatopoeia' is the use of words that:

  1. Have opposite meanings
  2. Sound like the thing they describe
  3. Are spelled the same forwards and backwards
  4. Are borrowed from other languages
Show answer

Answer: Sound like the thing they describe

Onomatopoeia: words whose pronunciation imitates the sound associated with the thing described. Examples: buzz (bees), hiss (snakes), sizzle (hot oil), splash (water), clang (metal), murmur (soft voices), cuckoo (the bird), drip, plop, crash, bang, thud.

Question 157easy

'Hyperbole' is:

  1. A mild or indirect expression for something unpleasant
  2. A deliberate, extreme exaggeration for effect
  3. A comparison using 'like' or 'as'
  4. Giving human qualities to inanimate things
Show answer

Answer: A deliberate, extreme exaggeration for effect

Hyperbole: deliberate exaggeration not meant to be taken literally, used for emphasis or humor. Examples: 'I've told you a million times', 'I'm so hungry I could eat a horse', 'This bag weighs a ton', 'I've been waiting forever'. Opposite: understatement (litotes).

Question 158easy

The word 'ambiguous' means:

  1. Very clear and certain
  2. Open to more than one interpretation; unclear
  3. Completely wrong
  4. Extremely simple
Show answer

Answer: Open to more than one interpretation; unclear

Ambiguous: open to more than one interpretation; having a double meaning; not clear. Synonyms: unclear, vague, equivocal, uncertain, open-ended. Antonyms: clear, unambiguous, explicit, definite. 'An ambiguous statement' can be understood in different ways.

Question 159hard

What is a 'malapropism'?

  1. The use of an unnecessarily long word
  2. The mistaken use of a word in place of a similar-sounding one
  3. A foreign word used in English
  4. A figure of speech using comparison
Show answer

Answer: The mistaken use of a word in place of a similar-sounding one

Malapropism: the mistaken use of a word in place of a similar-sounding one, often with a comic effect. Named after Mrs. Malaprop in Sheridan's play. Examples: 'He is the very pineapple of politeness' (instead of pinnacle), 'She is a bundle of many contradictions' (contradictions used when 'quirks' might be intended).

Question 160medium

Choose the sentence that uses 'affect' correctly:

  1. The new drug had a strange affect on the patient.
  2. The affect of the policy was immediate.
  3. The noise affected her concentration.
  4. She was affected by many affects.
Show answer

Answer: The noise affected her concentration.

'Affect' (verb): to have an influence on; to make a difference to. 'Effect' (noun): the result or outcome. 'The noise affected her concentration' = the noise had an influence on her concentration. 'The effect was immediate' = the result was immediate. Common mnemonic: RAVEN (Remember Affect Verb Effect Noun).

Question 161medium

Choose the sentence that uses 'effect' correctly:

  1. The medicine effected her in strange ways.
  2. The new law will effect immediate change.
  3. The medication had a calming effect on the patient.
  4. Weather can effect your mood significantly.
Show answer

Answer: The medication had a calming effect on the patient.

'Effect' (noun): result, outcome. 'Effect' (rare verb, formal): to bring about/cause. 'The medication had a calming effect' = noun (result/outcome). 'Affect' (common verb) = to influence. The rare verb use of 'effect': 'to effect change' = to bring about change (this is different from 'affect change').

Question 162easy

The word 'verbose' means:

  1. Concise and to the point
  2. Using or expressed in more words than are needed; wordy
  3. Completely silent
  4. Very energetic
Show answer

Answer: Using or expressed in more words than are needed; wordy

Verbose: using more words than necessary; wordy. Synonyms: wordy, long-winded, prolix, garrulous, rambling. Antonyms: concise, succinct, terse, brief, laconic. 'A verbose essay' wastes the reader's time with unnecessary words.

Question 163easy

The word 'eloquent' means:

  1. Speaking very quickly
  2. Fluent or persuasive in speaking or writing
  3. Speaking very softly
  4. Very angry
Show answer

Answer: Fluent or persuasive in speaking or writing

Eloquent: having or showing the ability to speak or write clearly and persuasively. An eloquent speech moves the audience. Synonyms: articulate, expressive, silver-tongued, persuasive. Antonyms: inarticulate, inexpressive, halting, tongue-tied.

Question 164easy

What is a 'rhetorical question'?

  1. A question asked in a debate
  2. A question asked for effect, not expecting an actual answer
  3. A question about the history of rhetoric
  4. A multiple-choice question
Show answer

Answer: A question asked for effect, not expecting an actual answer

Rhetorical question: asked for effect or to make a point, not to get information or an answer. Examples: 'Isn't it obvious?' 'Are you out of your mind?' 'What is the point of war?' Used in speeches and writing to engage the audience and emphasize a point.

Question 165hard

Which sentence demonstrates the correct use of 'as well as'?

  1. She, as well as her friends, are coming to the party.
  2. She, as well as her friends, is coming to the party.
  3. She as well as her friends come to the party.
  4. She as well as friends is coming.
Show answer

Answer: She, as well as her friends, is coming to the party.

When 'as well as' links a singular subject to additional elements, the verb agrees with the main subject (before 'as well as'): 'She, as well as her friends, is coming.' The phrase 'as well as her friends' is parenthetical (set off by commas) and doesn't affect the verb.

Question 166hard

Identify the error in: 'The reason for his failure is because he did not study.'

  1. 'failure' should be 'failing'
  2. 'is because' is redundant — use 'is that': 'The reason for his failure is that he did not study.'
  3. 'did not' should be 'hadn't'
  4. No error
Show answer

Answer: 'is because' is redundant — use 'is that': 'The reason for his failure is that he did not study.'

'The reason is because' is redundant — 'reason' and 'because' both indicate cause. Correct usage: 'The reason for his failure is that he did not study.' Similarly wrong: 'The reason why is because...' Correct: 'The reason why is that...' or just 'The reason is that...'

Question 167medium

The synonym of 'ephemeral' is:

  1. Permanent
  2. Lasting only a short time; transitory
  3. Very expensive

Answer: Lasting only a short time; transitory

Ephemeral: lasting for a very short time. Synonyms: transient, fleeting, momentary, short-lived, transitory. Antonyms: permanent, enduring, lasting, eternal, perpetual. 'Ephemeral beauty', 'ephemeral fashion trends' — things that don't last.

Question 168hard

The synonym of 'parsimonious' is:

Parsimonious: extremely unwilling to spend money; very stingy. Synonyms: stingy, miserly, tight-fisted, mean, niggardly. Antonyms: generous, lavish, extravagant, liberal. From Latin 'parsimonia' = thrift/economy (to an extreme).

Question 169easy

Benevolent: well-meaning and kindly; charitable. Its antonym is 'malevolent' (wishing harm to others). Other antonyms: cruel, malicious, unkind. Synonyms of benevolent: kind, charitable, generous, philanthropic, goodhearted.

Question 170easy

What does the phrase 'break the ice' mean?

  1. To skate on thin ice
  2. To do something that helps people who don't know each other begin to feel more comfortable
  3. To destroy something fragile
  4. To start an argument
Show answer

Answer: To do something that helps people who don't know each other begin to feel more comfortable

'Break the ice': to say or do something to relieve tension or awkwardness in a social situation, especially at the beginning. 'The host played a game to break the ice at the party.' Origin: ships breaking ice in frozen water to create a path.

Question 171easy

What does 'to sit on the fence' mean?

  2. To avoid committing to a side in a debate or controversy
  3. To be very stubborn

Answer: To avoid committing to a side in a debate or controversy

'To sit on the fence': to avoid taking a definite side or expressing a clear opinion about something controversial. 'When asked about the issue, she sat on the fence and refused to commit.' Synonyms: be non-committal, remain neutral, be on the fence.

Question 172easy

What does the phrase 'under the weather' mean?

  1. In bad weather conditions
  2. Feeling ill or unwell
  3. Being outdoors
  4. Worried about the weather
Show answer

Answer: Feeling ill or unwell

'Under the weather': feeling sick or unwell. 'I'm feeling a bit under the weather today, so I'll stay home.' Origin: sailors who were seasick were sent below deck (under the weather) where the weather was less rough. Common British/American idiom.

Question 173easy

What is an 'antonym'?

  1. A word with the same meaning as another
  2. A word opposite in meaning to another
  3. A word that sounds like another
  4. A word that looks like another
Show answer

Answer: A word opposite in meaning to another

Antonym: a word opposite in meaning to another. Examples: hot ↔ cold, fast ↔ slow, love ↔ hate, generous ↔ stingy, loquacious ↔ taciturn, ephemeral ↔ permanent. Types: gradable antonyms (hot/cold — degrees possible), complementary antonyms (alive/dead — no middle ground).

Question 174medium

In grammar, 'syntax' refers to:

  1. The study of word meanings
  2. The arrangement of words and phrases to create well-formed sentences
  3. The study of sounds in language
  4. The history of words (etymology)
Show answer

Answer: The arrangement of words and phrases to create well-formed sentences

Syntax: the set of rules governing the arrangement of words and phrases to form grammatically correct sentences in a language. Incorrect syntax creates confusion: 'Quickly the ran man' (incorrect) vs. 'The man ran quickly' (correct). Syntax differs from semantics (meaning) and phonology (sounds).

Question 175easy

The word 'indigenous' is a synonym for:

  1. Foreign and alien
  2. Native or originating in a particular place
  3. Rare and unusual
  4. Modern and new
Show answer

Answer: Native or originating in a particular place

Indigenous: originating or occurring naturally in a particular place; native. Synonyms: native, aboriginal, endemic, local. Antonyms: foreign, alien, exotic, imported. 'Indigenous plants of Tamil Nadu', 'indigenous knowledge of a community'. Often used in discussions of native peoples.

Question 176medium

Choose the sentence with correct subject-verb agreement:

  1. The mathematics department are launching a new course.
  2. The department of mathematics are launching a new course.
  3. The department of mathematics is launching a new course.
  4. The mathematics are very difficult for me.
Show answer

Answer: The department of mathematics is launching a new course.

The verb agrees with the main subject noun (not the noun in a prepositional phrase). 'The department' is the subject (singular) → 'is launching'. 'Of mathematics' is a prepositional phrase modifying 'department' — it does not affect the verb. Also, 'mathematics' as a subject takes a singular verb: 'Mathematics is difficult.'

Question 177medium

Choose the correct tag question for: 'She doesn't like coffee, ___?'

  1. does she
  2. doesn't she
  3. did she
  4. is she
Show answer

Answer: does she

Tag question formation: if the main clause is negative (doesn't), the tag is positive (does). If the main clause is positive (she likes), the tag is negative (doesn't she). Auxiliary verb matches the main verb tense. 'She doesn't like coffee, does she?'

Question 178medium

Choose the correct tag question for: 'They will arrive tomorrow, ___?'

  1. will they
  2. won't they
  3. are they
  4. aren't they
Show answer

Answer: won't they

Tag question rule: positive statement → negative tag. 'They will arrive' (positive) → 'won't they?' (negative tag). Use the same auxiliary verb: 'will' → 'won't'. 'She is happy, isn't she?', 'He has left, hasn't he?', 'They can swim, can't they?'

Question 179hard

Which sentence correctly uses the subjunctive mood?

  1. I suggest that she goes to the doctor.
  2. I suggest that she go to the doctor.
  3. I suggest that she will go to the doctor.
  4. I suggest that she went to the doctor.
Show answer

Answer: I suggest that she go to the doctor.

Subjunctive mood: used after verbs like suggest, recommend, insist, demand, require, propose + 'that' clause. In subjunctive, use the base form of the verb (without 's' for third person): 'I suggest that she GO.' NOT 'she goes'. Also: 'It is important that he BE there', 'I insist that they LEAVE'.

Question 180hard

The word 'enervate' means:

  1. To give energy and strength
  2. To weaken; to drain of energy or vitality
  3. To become angry
  4. To motivate and inspire
Show answer

Answer: To weaken; to drain of energy or vitality

Enervate: to weaken or drain of energy or vitality. Synonyms: debilitate, weaken, exhaust, enfeeble, sap. Antonyms: invigorate, energize, strengthen, fortify. Often confused with 'energize' (it means the opposite). 'The tropical heat enervated the hikers.'

Question 181medium

What does 'equivocal' mean?

  1. Perfectly clear and certain
  2. Open to more than one interpretation; ambiguous
  3. Very strong and forceful
  4. Completely honest
