Answer: Open to more than one interpretation; ambiguous

Equivocal: unclear; allowing of more than one interpretation; deliberately vague. Synonyms: ambiguous, unclear, vague, evasive. Antonyms: unequivocal, clear, definite, certain. 'An equivocal answer' is deliberately vague. 'Unequivocal support' = completely clear and firm support.

Question 182medium

What does 'verbose' have in common with 'loquacious'?

  1. Both mean silent
  2. Both mean very talkative/wordy
  3. Both mean truthful
  4. Both mean aggressive
Show answer

Answer: Both mean very talkative/wordy

Both 'verbose' and 'loquacious' mean using too many words or talking excessively. Verbose = using too many words in writing/speaking. Loquacious = very talkative. Synonyms for both: garrulous, prolix (for writing), rambling, long-winded. Antonyms: concise, brief, laconic, taciturn.

Question 183hard

The word 'erstwhile' means:

  1. Right now, currently
  2. Former; that was in the past
  3. In the future
  4. Very ancient
Show answer

Answer: Former; that was in the past

Erstwhile (adverb/adjective): former; in the past. 'His erstwhile friend became his rival.' Synonyms: former, ex-, one-time, previous, quondam. Used in formal writing. 'Erstwhile' implies that the quality or status no longer applies.

Question 184medium

'Cacophony' means:

  1. A pleasant combination of sounds
  2. A harsh, discordant mixture of sounds
  3. Complete silence
  4. A type of musical instrument
Show answer

Answer: A harsh, discordant mixture of sounds

Cacophony: a harsh, discordant mixture of sounds. From Greek 'kakos' (bad) + 'phone' (sound). Antonym: 'euphony' (pleasant combination of sounds). 'The cacophony of traffic' = harsh, unpleasant noise. Used in music, literature (jarring sounds for effect), and everyday language.

Question 185medium

'Euphony' means:

  1. A harsh and discordant mixture of sounds
  2. A pleasant-sounding combination of words or sounds
  3. Complete silence
  4. A very loud sound
Show answer

Answer: A pleasant-sounding combination of words or sounds

Euphony: quality of being pleasing to the ear; pleasant-sounding combination of words. From Greek 'eu' (good) + 'phone' (sound). Poets choose words for euphony. Antonym: 'cacophony'. Alliteration and assonance can create euphony in poetry.

Question 186hard

The term 'verisimilitude' in literature means:

  1. Using very difficult vocabulary
  2. The appearance of being true or real; lifelike quality
  3. An extreme exaggeration
  4. Comparison using 'like' or 'as'
Show answer

Answer: The appearance of being true or real; lifelike quality

Verisimilitude: the quality of seeming true or lifelike in a work of fiction or art. A novel has verisimilitude when its characters, events, and settings feel authentic. From Latin 'verus' (true) + 'similis' (like). Essential for realistic fiction.

Question 187medium

What is 'assonance' in poetry?

  1. Repetition of the same consonant sounds
  2. Repetition of similar vowel sounds in closely connected words
  3. Giving human qualities to inanimate objects
  4. A comparison using 'like' or 'as'
Show answer

Answer: Repetition of similar vowel sounds in closely connected words

Assonance: repetition of similar vowel sounds in nearby words, creating internal rhyme. Example: 'fleet feet sweep by sleeping geeks' (e sound). Compare: Alliteration = same consonant sounds at beginnings; Rhyme = same sound at end of lines; Assonance = vowel sounds repeated within.

Question 188hard

The grammatical term 'apposition' refers to:

  1. The position of an adjective before a noun
  2. A noun or noun phrase placed next to another to identify or describe it
  3. The opposite of a thesis statement
  4. The ending of a sentence
Show answer

Answer: A noun or noun phrase placed next to another to identify or describe it

Apposition: a noun or noun phrase placed beside another noun to identify or explain it. Example: 'My friend, the doctor, visited.' 'The doctor' is in apposition to 'my friend'. Appositives are set off by commas when non-restrictive. 'My brother Ravi is a teacher' (restrictive — no commas).

Question 189medium

What is the 'denouement' of a story?

  1. The main conflict or problem
  2. The final resolution after the climax, where loose ends are tied up
  3. The introduction of characters
  4. The rising action
Show answer

Answer: The final resolution after the climax, where loose ends are tied up

Denouement (from French): the final resolution of the plot, coming after the climax. Loose threads are tied up and the story concludes. Plot structure: Exposition → Rising Action → Climax → Falling Action → Denouement. 'Denouement' = literally 'untying' in French.

Question 190easy

A 'protagonist' in a story is:

  1. The villain or main opponent
  2. The main character or hero of the story
  3. A minor character
  4. The narrator
Show answer

Answer: The main character or hero of the story

Protagonist: the main character or hero of a story, who the narrative focuses on and who undergoes the most development. Antonym: 'antagonist' (the main opponent/villain). From Greek 'protos' (first) + 'agonistes' (actor/contestant).

Question 191easy

What is the 'antagonist' in a story?

  1. The hero or main character
  2. The character or force that opposes the protagonist
  3. The wise mentor
  4. The narrator of the story
Show answer

Answer: The character or force that opposes the protagonist

Antagonist: the character, group, or force that opposes the protagonist, creating conflict in the story. Classic antagonists: the Joker (vs Batman), Ravana (vs Rama), Iago (vs Othello). The antagonist can be a person, society, nature, or even the protagonist's inner conflict (man vs. self).

Question 192hard

'Stream of consciousness' as a literary technique refers to:

  1. A story set near a river
  2. An uninterrupted flow of a character's thoughts and feelings
  3. A type of poetry
  4. A form of journalism
Show answer

Answer: An uninterrupted flow of a character's thoughts and feelings

Stream of consciousness: a narrative technique showing the continuous flow of a character's inner thoughts and feelings, often in an unfiltered, loosely structured way. Used by Virginia Woolf, James Joyce, William Faulkner. Famous example: Joyce's 'Ulysses', Woolf's 'Mrs Dalloway'.

Question 193medium

A 'haiku' is a form of Japanese poetry characterized by:

  1. Four stanzas and a rhyme scheme
  2. Three lines with 5-7-5 syllable structure, often about nature
  3. Fourteen lines in iambic pentameter
  4. Heroic couplets in pairs
Show answer

Answer: Three lines with 5-7-5 syllable structure, often about nature

Haiku: traditional Japanese poem with 3 lines in 5-7-5 syllable structure, typically about nature or a moment. Famous haiku by Matsuo Bashō: 'An old silent pond / A frog jumps into the pond / Splash! Silence again.' Haiku are concise and evocative.

Question 194medium

A 'sonnet' is:

  1. A 16-line poem with alternating rhyme
  2. A 14-line poem with a specific rhyme scheme (Petrarchan or Shakespearean)
  3. A prose poem without fixed structure
  4. A poem about heroes and battles
Show answer

Answer: A 14-line poem with a specific rhyme scheme (Petrarchan or Shakespearean)

Sonnet: a 14-line poem written in iambic pentameter. Petrarchan (Italian): 8 lines (octave) + 6 lines (sestet), rhyme scheme ABBAABBA CDECDE. Shakespearean (English): 3 quatrains + 1 couplet, rhyme scheme ABAB CDCD EFEF GG. Shakespeare wrote 154 sonnets.

Question 195easy

Choose the sentence with the correctly used idiom:

  1. She decided to take the bull by the horns and address the problem directly.
  2. She took the bull by the tail and ran away from the problem.
  3. She grabbed the bull by the ears to confront the issue.
  4. She took the horse by the horns to solve the problem.
Show answer

Answer: She decided to take the bull by the horns and address the problem directly.

'Take the bull by the horns' = to face a difficult situation directly and with courage, without avoiding it. 'She decided to take the bull by the horns and address the problem directly.' The correct animal (bull) and body part (horns) must be used for the idiom to be correct.

Question 196easy

What is the correct meaning of 'to burn bridges'?

  1. To be an engineer
  2. To permanently destroy a relationship or opportunity
  3. To help others in need
  4. To start a new journey

Answer: To permanently destroy a relationship or opportunity

'To burn bridges': to permanently damage or destroy a relationship or opportunity by behaving badly, making enemies, or doing something unforgivable. 'He burned his bridges at the company by quitting rudely.' Military origin: destroying bridges behind you to prevent retreat or enemy advance.

Question 197medium

The word 'laconic' means:

  1. Very talkative
  2. Brief and concise; using few words
  3. Very emotional
  4. Confused and uncertain
Show answer

Answer: Brief and concise; using few words

Laconic: using very few words; brief and concise. From 'Laconia' (Sparta) — Spartans were famously brief in speech. 'A laconic reply' is short and to the point. Synonyms: concise, terse, brief, succinct. Antonyms: verbose, loquacious, garrulous, prolix.

Question 198easy

Which of the following is a 'compound noun'?

  1. Quickly
  2. Sunflower
  3. Beautiful
  4. Running
Show answer

Answer: Sunflower

Compound noun: a noun formed from two or more words joined together. 'Sunflower' = sun + flower. Other compound nouns: football, bedroom, toothbrush, butterfly, earthquake, bookstore, raincoat, notebook, lighthouse. They can be written as one word, hyphenated, or two words.

Question 199easy

The word 'notorious' means:

  1. Famous for good reasons
  2. Famous or well known for a bad quality or deed
  3. Unknown and obscure
  4. Very ordinary
Show answer

Answer: Famous or well known for a bad quality or deed

Notorious: famous for something bad; having a bad reputation. Synonyms: infamous, disreputable, ill-famed. Antonyms: unknown, obscure, reputable. 'A notorious criminal' is well-known for criminal activity. Note: 'famous' is neutral or positive; 'notorious' and 'infamous' are specifically for bad fame.

Question 200medium

Which word is correctly hyphenated?

  1. well-known author
  2. well known author
  3. wellknown author
  4. well-known-author
Show answer

Answer: well-known author

Compound adjectives that precede a noun are hyphenated: 'well-known author', 'high-quality product', 'long-term investment'. When they follow the noun, no hyphen: 'The author is well known.' Rule: hyphenate attributive (before noun) compound modifiers, but not predicative (after noun).

Question 201medium

Choose the sentence using 'while' correctly to show contrast:

  1. While I was eating, she called.
  2. While she loves reading, her brother prefers sports.
  3. I was sleeping while the alarm rang.
  4. He arrived while we were leaving.
Show answer

Answer: While she loves reading, her brother prefers sports.

'While' can show: (1) simultaneous actions: 'While I was eating, she called'; (2) contrast: 'While she loves reading, her brother prefers sports.' The contrast use is like 'whereas' or 'although'. All options are grammatically correct, but option B specifically demonstrates the contrast/concession use.

Question 202hard

What is 'metonymy'?

  1. Exaggerating something to extreme
  2. Substituting the name of an attribute for the thing itself (e.g., 'crown' for 'king')
  3. Giving human qualities to inanimate things
  4. A comparison using 'like' or 'as'
Show answer

Answer: Substituting the name of an attribute for the thing itself (e.g., 'crown' for 'king')

Metonymy: a figure of speech where something is referred to by the name of something closely associated with it. Examples: 'The White House announced' (= the President), 'The pen is mightier than the sword' (= writing vs. violence), 'Hollywood' (= the film industry), 'Downing Street' (= the UK government).

Question 203hard

'Circumlocution' means:

  1. Using very direct language
  2. The use of many words where fewer would do; roundabout expression
  3. Writing in a circular pattern
  4. A type of formal address
Show answer

Answer: The use of many words where fewer would do; roundabout expression

Circumlocution: using many words to express something that could be said with fewer words; verbose or evasive expression. From Latin 'circum' (around) + 'locutio' (speaking). 'Instead of saying he died, he used circumlocution: he passed on to the other side.' Similar: periphrasis, verbosity.

Question 204hard

Which sentence contains an 'absolute phrase' (nominative absolute)?

  1. The work finished, they went home.
  2. He finished the work.
  3. They went home after work.
  4. After finishing, they left.
Show answer

Answer: The work finished, they went home.

An absolute phrase (nominative absolute) consists of a noun/pronoun + participle (and often other modifiers) that modifies the entire main clause. 'The work finished, they went home.' = 'The work being finished, they went home.' It is not grammatically connected to the main clause by a conjunction.

Question 205hard

The word 'pedantic' is best described as:

  1. Caring too little about rules
  2. Overly concerned with minor details and formal rules, often in a tiresome way
  3. Very creative and original
  4. Simple and easy to understand
Show answer

Answer: Overly concerned with minor details and formal rules, often in a tiresome way

Pedantic: being overly concerned with minor details, formalities, or book learning in a way that is tiresome or unnecessary. A pedantic teacher corrects students on the smallest grammatical points. Synonyms: nitpicking, fussy, punctilious, hair-splitting, schoolmasterly.

  4. Highly intelligent

Answer: Deserving or causing public disgrace or shame

Ignominious: deserving or causing public disgrace or shame. Synonyms: shameful, humiliating, disgraceful, dishonorable. Antonyms: glorious, honorable, distinguished. 'An ignominious defeat', 'an ignominious exit from power'. Related: ignominy (noun) = public shame.

Question 207medium

Select the correctly spelled word:

  1. Emminent
  2. Emminant
  3. Eminent
  4. Emmenant
Show answer

Answer: Eminent

'Eminent' = famous and respected, especially in a particular field. Common misspelling: 'emminent' (double m). Also confused with: 'imminent' (about to happen soon) and 'immanent' (existing within, inherent). Eminent → E + min + ent. Think: 'em-in-ent' — someone who stands out/above.

Question 208easy

Which of the following words is an 'abstract noun'?

  1. Table
  2. Happiness
  3. Stone
  4. Mountain
Show answer

Answer: Happiness

Abstract noun: a noun that refers to ideas, qualities, or states that cannot be physically touched or seen. 'Happiness', 'justice', 'freedom', 'courage', 'love', 'honesty', 'knowledge', 'beauty'. Concrete nouns: table, stone, mountain — physical objects that can be perceived by the senses.

'An apple a day keeps the doctor away' is an example of:

  3. A simile
  4. A hyperbole

Proverb: a short, well-known saying that expresses a general truth or piece of advice. Proverbs are traditional, widely accepted wisdom. This proverb advises eating healthily. Idiom: a phrase with a non-literal meaning. Simile: comparison with 'like' or 'as'.

Question 210easy

'Every cloud has a silver lining': every difficult or negative situation has some positive aspect or opportunity, if you look for it. Similar: 'When one door closes, another opens.' It encourages optimism and looking for good even in bad circumstances.

Question 211easy

  2. People who start their work early are more likely to succeed

  4. Always be punctual for meetings

'The early bird catches the worm': people who start early, arrive first, or are most prepared are most likely to be successful and take advantage of available opportunities. Lesson: diligence and timeliness lead to success.

Question 212easy

The word 'perspire' is a formal/euphemistic synonym for:

  1. To breathe
  2. To sweat
  3. To blink
  4. To cough
Show answer

Answer: To sweat

'Perspire' is a formal/polite substitute for 'sweat'. Euphemisms replace unpleasant or taboo words with milder alternatives. 'Perspire' is from Latin 'perspirare' (to breathe through). Similar: 'pass away' (die), 'between jobs' (unemployed), 'restroom' (toilet).

Question 213medium

Choose the sentence that uses a 'gerund phrase' correctly:

  1. To study every night is her routine.
  2. Studying every night is her routine.
  3. She studies every night is her routine.
  4. Every night studying is her routine.
Show answer

Answer: Studying every night is her routine.

Gerund phrase: a gerund (verb+ing) plus any modifiers or complements, functioning as a noun. 'Studying every night' is a gerund phrase acting as the subject of the sentence. 'To study every night' is an infinitive phrase (also correct as a subject), but the question asks specifically for a gerund phrase.

Question 214medium

Which word correctly fills the blank? 'She was completely ___ by the complexity of the task.'

  1. Overwhelmed
  2. Overwhelming
  3. Overwhelm
  4. Overwheming
Show answer

Answer: Overwhelmed

'Overwhelmed' (past participle used as adjective) describes how a person feels: 'She was overwhelmed.' Compare: 'The task was overwhelming' (present participle as adjective = describes the thing causing the feeling). Person = -ed adjective; thing causing feeling = -ing adjective. 'She felt overwhelmed (not overwhelming).'

Question 215medium

The word 'panacea' means:

  1. A type of tropical disease
  2. A solution or remedy for all problems or difficulties
  3. A medical examination
  4. A type of medicine for headaches
Show answer

Answer: A solution or remedy for all problems or difficulties

Panacea: a supposed cure for all diseases or problems; a universal remedy. From Greek: Panacea was the goddess of universal remedy. 'There is no panacea for poverty.' Used figuratively: 'Technology is not a panacea for all social problems.' Often used to caution against oversimplified solutions.

Question 216hard

What is the meaning of 'contiguous'?

  1. Sharing a common border; touching along a boundary
  2. Very controversial
  3. Contagious and infectious
  4. Distant and far apart
Show answer

Answer: Sharing a common border; touching along a boundary

Contiguous: sharing a common border; directly touching; adjacent. 'India and Bangladesh are contiguous countries.' The contiguous United States = the 48 states that share borders (excluding Alaska and Hawaii). Synonyms: adjacent, bordering, touching, neighboring.

Question 217hard

What is the meaning of 'fortuitous'?

  1. Strong and brave
  2. Happening by chance, especially a lucky chance
  3. Planned and deliberate
  4. Very unfortunate
Show answer

Answer: Happening by chance, especially a lucky chance

Fortuitous: happening by chance, often with positive results; accidental but fortunate. 'Their meeting was entirely fortuitous.' Synonyms: accidental, chance, serendipitous. Note: 'fortuitous' does NOT mean 'fortunate' (lucky) though they are often confused. It specifically means 'happening by chance'.

Question 218easy

Select the sentence free of errors:

  1. She is more smarter than her sister.
  2. She is smarter than her sister.
  3. She is most smartest than her sister.
  4. She is much more smarter than her sister.
Show answer

Answer: She is smarter than her sister.

For one-syllable and many two-syllable adjectives, add '-er' for comparative: smart → smarter. Never use 'more' with '-er' comparatives ('more smarter' is double comparison — wrong). 'She is smarter than her sister.' For adjectives of 3+ syllables: 'more intelligent'.

Question 219medium

Which sentence correctly uses 'despite' vs 'in spite of'?

  1. Despite of the heavy rain, they continued the match.
  2. Despite the heavy rain, they continued the match.
  3. In spite the heavy rain, they continued.
  4. Despite of the rain, they played.
Show answer

Answer: Despite the heavy rain, they continued the match.

'Despite' and 'in spite of' have the same meaning (used to show contrast with an obstacle). 'Despite' NEVER takes 'of': 'despite the rain' (NOT 'despite of the rain'). 'In spite of' always takes 'of': 'in spite of the rain'. Both are followed by a noun or gerund, not a clause (use 'although/though' for clauses).

Question 220medium

'Hubris' means:

  1. Great humility
  2. Excessive pride or self-confidence that leads to downfall
  3. A type of Greek food
  4. Extraordinary talent
Show answer

Answer: Excessive pride or self-confidence that leads to downfall

Hubris: excessive pride or overconfidence, especially when it leads to tragedy or downfall. In Greek tragedy, hubris leads heroes to defy the gods and meet their doom. Modern usage: 'The company's hubris — believing they could never fail — led to their bankruptcy.' Antonym: humility.

Question 221hard

The word 'euphemism' is the opposite of:

  1. Understatement
  2. Dysphemism (harsh, unpleasant expression for something taboo)
  3. Litotes
  4. Simile
Show answer

Answer: Dysphemism (harsh, unpleasant expression for something taboo)

Euphemism: a mild or polite expression substituted for an unpleasant one ('passed away' for 'died'). Dysphemism: an intentionally harsh or blunt expression used instead of a more neutral one. Dysphemism is used to shock, insult, or express strong emotion. Opposite of euphemism.

Question 222medium

Identify the sentence in 'third conditional' form:

  1. If you heat water, it boils.
  2. If I were rich, I would travel.
  3. If she had studied, she would have passed.
  4. If he studies, he will succeed.
Show answer

Answer: If she had studied, she would have passed.

Third conditional: if + past perfect, would have + past participle. Used for unreal or impossible past situations and their hypothetical results. 'If she had studied (she didn't), she would have passed (but she didn't).' It expresses regret about past actions.

Question 223medium

The word 'ubiquitous' means:

  2. Present or seeming to be everywhere at the same time
  3. Very rare and unusual
  4. Extremely powerful

Answer: Present or seeming to be everywhere at the same time

Ubiquitous: present or appearing everywhere simultaneously. Synonyms: omnipresent, everywhere, pervasive, universal. 'Smartphones have become ubiquitous in modern life.' From Latin 'ubique' = everywhere. The antonym is 'rare', 'scarce', or 'uncommon'.

Question 224easy

What is the one-word substitute for 'a book written by an author about their own life'?

  1. Biography
  2. Autobiography
  3. Memoir
  4. Diary
Show answer

Answer: Autobiography

Autobiography: a written account of a person's own life, written by themselves. Biography: the story of someone's life written by another person. Memoir: focuses on specific experiences or a period of one's life (less comprehensive than autobiography). Diary: daily personal record.

Question 225medium

  2. Epitaph
  3. Epigram
  4. Epithet
Show answer

Answer: Epitaph

Epitaph: a phrase or statement written on a tombstone. Epigraph: a quote at the beginning of a book. Epigram: a short witty saying. Epithet: an adjective or phrase describing a person's character ('Alexander the Great'). Famous epitaph: 'Here lies one whose name was writ in water' (Keats).

Question 226medium

  2. Devotee
  3. Zealot
  4. Fanatic
Show answer

Answer: Devotee

Devotee: a person who is very enthusiastic and committed to a particular activity, cause, or person. 'A devotee of classical music.' More positive than 'zealot' (which implies extreme, often dangerous dedication) or 'fanatic' (extreme, unreasonable). Also: one devoted to a religious faith.

Question 227hard

Which sentence uses 'whom' correctly?

  1. Whom is calling at this hour?
  2. The person who you trust most may let you down.
  3. She is the one whom I have always admired.
  4. Whom do you think you are?
Show answer

Answer: She is the one whom I have always admired.

'Whom' is used as an object pronoun. 'She is the one whom I have always admired.' Test: 'I have admired her/him' (object) → use 'whom'. 'Whom is calling?' (incorrect — subject position needs 'who': 'Who is calling?'). 'Whom do you think you are?' (subject of 'are' → should be 'who').

Question 228easy

'Bury the hatchet': to end a disagreement or conflict; to make peace. 'After years of rivalry, the two companies finally buried the hatchet.' Origin: Native American tradition of literally burying weapons (hatchets/tomahawks) to symbolize peace. Antonym: 'to pick a fight', 'to fan the flames'.

'Throw in the towel': to give up; to admit defeat or abandon a contest. 'After three failed attempts, she finally threw in the towel.' Origin: boxing — a trainer throws a towel into the ring to stop a fight and concede defeat. Synonyms: give up, surrender, concede, wave the white flag.

Question 230easy

'Pull someone's leg': to tease or joke with someone; to say something untrue in a playful way. 'I'm just pulling your leg — the exam wasn't that hard.' Synonyms: kid, tease, joke, wind up. 'Are you pulling my leg?' = 'Are you joking with me?'

Question 231medium

What is the grammatical function of 'although' in: 'Although it was raining, we went for a walk.'?

  1. Coordinating conjunction
  2. Subordinating conjunction
  3. Preposition
  4. Adverb
Show answer

Answer: Subordinating conjunction

'Although' is a subordinating conjunction that introduces a dependent (subordinate) clause and connects it to the independent (main) clause. It shows contrast or concession. Other subordinating conjunctions of contrast: though, even though, while, whereas, despite the fact that.

Question 232medium

The word 'abscond' means:

  1. To stay and face consequences
  2. To leave hurriedly and secretly, typically to avoid detection
  3. To ask for forgiveness
  4. To make a formal complaint
Show answer

Answer: To leave hurriedly and secretly, typically to avoid detection

Abscond: to leave hurriedly and secretly, usually to escape from custody or avoid legal consequences. 'The embezzler absconded with company funds.' Synonyms: flee, escape, bolt, run away, make off. Used in legal contexts: 'absconding from prison', 'absconding with money'.

Question 233easy

  2. Briefly; in a very concise way
  3. In great detail
  4. In a confused manner
Show answer

'In a nutshell': expressed in a very concise way; briefly and simply. 'In a nutshell, the project failed because of poor planning.' Origin: refers to putting a large amount of information into a very small space (the size of a nutshell). Synonyms: briefly, in short, to sum up, in a word.

Question 234easy

What is an 'oxymoron'? Give an example.

  1. A figure of speech using 'like' or 'as'; e.g., 'white as snow'
  2. A combination of contradictory terms; e.g., 'deafening silence'
  3. Exaggeration for effect; e.g., 'I've told you a million times'
  4. Giving human qualities to things; e.g., 'the sun smiled'
Show answer

Answer: A combination of contradictory terms; e.g., 'deafening silence'

Oxymoron: a figure of speech that combines two seemingly contradictory terms for expressive effect. 'Deafening silence' (silence cannot deafen, yet feels overpowering). Other oxymorons: bittersweet, living death, open secret, clearly confused, awfully good, alone together, original copy.

Question 235hard

Identify the error in the following sentence: 'Between the three candidates, she is the best.'

  1. 'best' should be 'better' when referring to two items, and 'best' is for three or more — so 'best' is correct here
  2. 'Between' should be 'Among' — use 'between' for two; 'among' for three or more
  3. 'three candidates' should be 'three candidate'
  4. No error
Show answer

Answer: 'Between' should be 'Among' — use 'between' for two; 'among' for three or more

Traditional grammar rule: 'Between' is used for exactly two: 'between you and me', 'between the two of us'. 'Among' is used for three or more: 'among the three candidates', 'among the group'. 'Between' with three or more is common in informal speech but technically incorrect in formal/exam contexts.

Question 236easy

The word 'lethargic' means:

  1. Energetic and active
  2. Feeling slow, tired, and sluggish; lacking energy
  3. Very angry
  4. Extremely happy
Show answer

Answer: Feeling slow, tired, and sluggish; lacking energy

Lethargic: feeling sluggish, lacking energy, slow, and apathetic. Synonyms: sluggish, drowsy, dull, inactive, torpid. Antonyms: energetic, alert, lively, active. 'She felt lethargic after a heavy meal.' Related: lethargy (noun) = state of extreme tiredness and lack of energy.

Question 237easy

The word 'pragmatic' means:

  1. Overly idealistic
  2. Dealing with things sensibly and realistically; practical rather than theoretical
  3. Very emotional
  4. Completely inflexible
Show answer

Answer: Dealing with things sensibly and realistically; practical rather than theoretical

Pragmatic: practical, realistic, and concerned with results rather than ideology or theory. Synonyms: practical, realistic, sensible, matter-of-fact, down-to-earth. Antonyms: idealistic, utopian, impractical. 'A pragmatic approach' focuses on what works in the real world.

Question 238medium

What is the one-word substitute for 'murder of a king'?

  1. Genocide
  2. Regicide
  3. Patricide
  4. Fratricide
Show answer

Answer: Regicide

Regicide: the killing of a king. Patricide: killing of one's father. Matricide: killing of one's mother. Fratricide: killing of one's brother. Genocide: killing of a large group of people (especially an ethnic group). Homicide: killing of a human. Infanticide: killing of an infant.

Question 239easy

What is the one-word substitute for 'a place where bees are kept'?

  1. Kennel
  2. Apiary
  3. Aviary
  4. Aquarium
Show answer

Answer: Apiary

Apiary: a place where bees are kept; a collection of beehives. Kennel: where dogs are kept. Aviary: where birds are kept (large enclosure). Aquarium: where fish/aquatic animals are kept. Stable: horses. Pigsty: pigs. Hutch: rabbits. Den: lions. Burrow: rabbits/foxes underground.

Question 240easy

What is the one-word substitute for 'fear of water'?

  1. Hydrophilia
  2. Hydrophobia (or aquaphobia)
  3. Claustrophobia
  4. Xenophobia
Show answer

Answer: Hydrophobia (or aquaphobia)

Hydrophobia (aquaphobia): extreme fear of water. Also a symptom of rabies. Other phobias: claustrophobia (closed spaces), agoraphobia (open/crowded spaces), acrophobia (heights), xenophobia (strangers/foreigners), arachnophobia (spiders), nyctophobia (darkness), pyrophobia (fire).

Question 241easy

Which pair are antonyms?

  1. Benevolent / Charitable
  2. Transient / Permanent
  3. Lucid / Clear
  4. Garrulous / Talkative
Show answer

Answer: Transient / Permanent

Antonyms are words with opposite meanings. 'Transient' (short-lived, temporary) and 'permanent' (lasting indefinitely) are antonyms. The other pairs are synonyms: benevolent/charitable (both = kind), lucid/clear (both = understandable), garrulous/talkative (both = very talkative).

Question 242medium

'Limericks' are humorous poems with which rhyme scheme?

  1. ABCDE (no rhyme)
  2. ABAB
  3. AABBA
  4. ABBA
Show answer

Answer: AABBA

Limerick: a humorous 5-line poem with AABBA rhyme scheme. Lines 1, 2, 5 rhyme with each other (A). Lines 3, 4 rhyme with each other (B). Lines 1, 2, 5 are longer; lines 3, 4 are shorter. Famous example: 'There once was a man from Nantucket...' Popularized by Edward Lear.

Question 243medium

The word 'abate' means:

  1. To increase rapidly
  2. To become less intense or widespread; to subside
  3. To remain unchanged
  4. To completely stop suddenly
Show answer

Answer: To become less intense or widespread; to subside

Abate: to become less intense, widespread, or severe; to decrease. 'The storm abated by morning.' Synonyms: subside, diminish, lessen, ease, wane, decrease. Antonyms: intensify, increase, escalate, worsen. 'The noise abated' = the noise got quieter/less.

Question 244easy

Choose the sentence with the correct punctuation for a list:

  1. We need: milk bread and butter
  2. We need milk, bread, and butter.
  3. We need milk bread, and butter.
  4. We need: milk, bread, and, butter.
Show answer

Answer: We need milk, bread, and butter.

Items in a series are separated by commas. The Oxford comma (before 'and' in the final item: 'milk, bread, and butter') is recommended in formal writing to avoid ambiguity. Do NOT put a colon after 'need' when it directly precedes the list (colons follow complete clauses).

Question 245medium

What is the meaning of 'esprit de corps'?

  1. Courage and bravery in battle
  2. A feeling of pride, fellowship, and loyalty shared by members of a group
  3. Strict military discipline
  4. A type of French dance
Show answer

Answer: A feeling of pride, fellowship, and loyalty shared by members of a group

Esprit de corps (French): the common spirit of comradeship, pride, enthusiasm, and devotion to the group that exists among its members. Used for teams, military units, workplaces. 'The team's esprit de corps carried them through difficult times.' Literally: 'spirit of the body'.

Question 246medium

What is the meaning of 'bona fide'?

  1. Acting in bad faith
  2. Genuine, real, or sincere; made in good faith
  3. A legal document
  4. A formal agreement
Show answer

Answer: Genuine, real, or sincere; made in good faith

'Bona fide' (Latin: 'good faith'): genuine, authentic, or sincere; done honestly and without intention to deceive. 'A bona fide offer', 'a bona fide member'. Used as adjective: 'a bona fide emergency'. Opposite: fraudulent, fake, pretended. 'Bona fide' is often used in legal and business contexts.

Question 247medium

What is the meaning of 'de facto'?

  1. By law and official recognition
  2. Existing or occurring in reality, even if not officially recognized
  3. In theory only
  4. According to tradition
Show answer

Answer: Existing or occurring in reality, even if not officially recognized

'De facto' (Latin: 'from the fact'): in practice or reality, even if not officially established by law. 'De facto leader' = acts as leader even without official title. Contrasted with 'de jure' (by law, official recognition). 'He was de facto ruler though he held no formal title.'

Question 248easy

Which word is a synonym for 'adamant'?

  1. Flexible
  2. Unyielding and firm
  3. Generous
  4. Confused
Show answer

Answer: Unyielding and firm

Adamant: refusing to be persuaded; completely determined. Synonyms: firm, resolute, unyielding, steadfast, unwavering. Antonyms: flexible, persuadable, yielding. 'She was adamant that she would not change her decision.'

Question 249easy

The word 'forthcoming' means:

  1. Shy and reserved
  2. About to happen; willing to help and give information
  3. Very old-fashioned
  4. Impossible to achieve
Show answer

Answer: About to happen; willing to help and give information

Forthcoming: (1) about to happen or appear soon ('the forthcoming elections'); (2) willing to divulge information ('he was not forthcoming about his plans'). Synonyms: imminent, upcoming, cooperative, open. Antonyms: past, reticent, secretive.

Question 250easy

A 'bibliography' is placed:

  1. At the beginning of a book
  2. In the middle of a chapter
  3. At the end of a book or research paper, listing sources used
  4. On the cover page
Show answer

Answer: At the end of a book or research paper, listing sources used

Bibliography: a list of all sources (books, articles, websites) consulted while writing a book or paper. Placed at the end. Different from 'Works Cited' (only sources quoted) vs 'bibliography' (all sources consulted). Essential for academic writing to avoid plagiarism.

Question 251hard

Which sentence uses an 'infinitive phrase' as an adjective?

  1. She wants to become a doctor.
  2. He bought a book to read on the train.
  3. To exercise daily is important.
  4. She tried to finish the work quickly.
Show answer

Answer: He bought a book to read on the train.

An infinitive phrase used as an adjective modifies a noun. 'A book to read on the train' — 'to read on the train' modifies 'book' (tells us what kind of book). In option A, 'to become a doctor' is a noun phrase (object of wants). In C, 'to exercise daily' is a noun phrase (subject).

Question 252medium

What is a 'tautology'?

  1. A figure of speech using contradiction
  2. Saying the same thing twice using different words; needless repetition
  3. A type of question asked for effect
  4. Using a part to represent the whole
Show answer

Answer: Saying the same thing twice using different words; needless repetition

Tautology: saying the same thing twice in different words, needlessly repeating the same idea. Examples: 'free gift' (a gift is always free), 'past history' (history is always past), 'future plans' (plans are always for the future), 'end result', 'ATM machine' (Automatic Teller Machine machine).

Question 253easy

Choose the sentence with the correct form of 'there', 'their', or 'they're':

  1. They're going to submit there assignment tomorrow.
  2. Their going to submit they're assignment.
  3. They're going to submit their assignment tomorrow.
  4. There going to submit their assignment tomorrow.
Show answer

Answer: They're going to submit their assignment tomorrow.

'They're' = they are (contraction). 'Their' = belonging to them (possessive). 'There' = that place or an expletive. 'They're going to submit their assignment' = 'They are going to submit the assignment belonging to them.' One of the most common homophone errors in English.

Question 254easy

What does 'to bite the bullet' mean?

  1. To eat quickly
  2. To endure a difficult situation with courage and stoicism
  3. To make a rash decision
  4. To speak harshly to someone

Answer: To endure a difficult situation with courage and stoicism

'Bite the bullet': to endure a painful or difficult situation bravely and without complaining. 'She bit the bullet and went through the painful procedure without anaesthesia.' Origin: soldiers bit a bullet during surgery without anaesthesia in battles. Synonyms: grin and bear it, tough it out.

Question 255easy

'Wear your heart on your sleeve': to openly display one's emotions rather than hiding them. 'He wears his heart on his sleeve — everyone can tell when he's upset.' From Shakespeare's Othello. Antonym: 'to keep a stiff upper lip' (hide emotions).

Question 256easy

What does 'to cry wolf' mean?

  1. To imitate animal sounds
  2. To raise a false alarm; to call for help when it's not needed
  3. To complain constantly
  4. To be very loud
Show answer

Answer: To raise a false alarm; to call for help when it's not needed

'Cry wolf': to raise a false alarm; to call for help when no help is actually needed, which eventually causes people to ignore genuine calls for help. From Aesop's fable 'The Boy Who Cried Wolf'. 'He cried wolf so often that nobody believed him when there was a real emergency.'

Question 257easy

Aloof: not friendly or forthcoming; distant; keeping oneself apart from others. 'She remained aloof at the party, not engaging with anyone.' Synonyms: distant, detached, remote, reserved, standoffish. Antonyms: friendly, warm, approachable, sociable.

Question 261medium

The figure of speech 'anaphora' involves:

  1. Repetition of words at the end of successive clauses
  2. Repetition of a word or phrase at the beginning of successive clauses
  3. Using contradictory terms together
  4. Comparing two unlike things
Show answer

Answer: Repetition of a word or phrase at the beginning of successive clauses

Anaphora: repetition of a word or phrase at the beginning of successive clauses for emphasis. Famous example: Martin Luther King Jr.'s 'I have a dream' speech. Another: Churchill's 'We shall fight on the beaches, we shall fight on the landing grounds, we shall fight in the fields...'

Question 262hard

The figure of speech 'epistrophe' (or epiphora) involves:

  1. Repetition of words at the beginning of clauses
  2. Repetition of a word or phrase at the end of successive clauses
  3. Using human qualities for objects
  4. Exaggerating facts
Show answer

Answer: Repetition of a word or phrase at the end of successive clauses

Epistrophe: repetition of a word or phrase at the end of successive clauses. Example: 'We will not tire, we will not falter, and we will not fail.' (George W. Bush). Another: 'When I was a child, I spoke like a child, I thought like a child, I reasoned like a child.' (1 Corinthians 13:11)

Question 263easy

Which word correctly fills the blank? 'The film was so ___ that the audience fell asleep.'

  1. Fascinating
  2. Tedious
  3. Exciting
  4. Gripping
Show answer

Answer: Tedious

'Tedious' means too long, slow, or dull; monotonously tiresome. 'The film was so tedious that the audience fell asleep.' Synonyms: boring, dull, monotonous, dreary. Antonyms: fascinating, exciting, gripping, engaging.

Question 264medium

The word 'vicarious' means:

  1. Direct and firsthand
  2. Experienced through the feelings or actions of another person
  3. Very risky
  4. Extremely important
Show answer

Answer: Experienced through the feelings or actions of another person

Vicarious: experienced in the imagination through the feelings or actions of another person. 'Vicarious pleasure', 'vicarious thrill', 'vicarious living through your children'. From Latin 'vicarius' = substitute/deputy. Contrast: direct experience vs. vicarious experience.

Question 265medium

Choose the sentence that is NOT a run-on sentence:

  1. She was tired she went to bed.
  2. It was cold outside we stayed home.
  3. Although it was cold, we went for a walk.
  4. He likes coffee she prefers tea.
Show answer

Answer: Although it was cold, we went for a walk.

Run-on sentence: two or more independent clauses joined without proper punctuation or coordinating conjunction. 'Although it was cold, we went for a walk' is correct — 'although' makes the first clause dependent. The others are run-on sentences that need a semicolon, period, or coordinating conjunction.

Question 266medium

A 'sentence fragment' is:

  1. A very short complete sentence
  2. An incomplete sentence that lacks a subject, verb, or complete thought
  3. A sentence with a spelling error
  4. A sentence that is too long
Show answer

Answer: An incomplete sentence that lacks a subject, verb, or complete thought

Sentence fragment: an incomplete sentence that lacks a subject, a verb, or a complete thought. Examples of fragments: 'Running down the street.' (no subject), 'Because she was tired.' (incomplete thought — doesn't say what happened because of the tiredness). Fragments must be corrected in formal writing.

Question 267easy

Select the sentence with the correct use of 'its' vs 'it's':

  1. Its important to study every day.
  2. The company lost it's reputation.
  3. The dog wagged its tail happily.
  4. Its' raining outside.
Show answer

Answer: The dog wagged its tail happily.

'Its' (no apostrophe) = possessive pronoun ('the dog's tail' = 'its tail'). 'It's' (with apostrophe) = 'it is' or 'it has'. 'The dog wagged its tail' = correct. 'It's important' = 'It is important'. 'Its'' does not exist. Note: possessive pronouns (his, her, its, our, their) NEVER have apostrophes.

Question 268easy

  2. To say something embarrassing, tactless, or offensive by accident
  3. To remain silent
  4. To take a firm stand

Answer: To say something embarrassing, tactless, or offensive by accident

'Put your foot in your mouth': to say something accidentally that is embarrassing, offensive, or socially inappropriate. 'He really put his foot in his mouth when he asked her when her baby was due — she wasn't pregnant.' Synonyms: blunder, gaffe, social faux pas.

Question 269easy

'Get cold feet': to suddenly lose confidence or courage about something you were going to do; to become nervous and hesitant at the last moment. 'She got cold feet before the job interview and almost didn't go.' Synonyms: lose nerve, waver, have second thoughts.

Question 270easy

What does 'actions speak louder than words' mean?

  1. Loud actions are better than speech
  2. What you do is more important and convincing than what you say
  3. Speaking is better than acting
  4. Words are more powerful than actions

Answer: What you do is more important and convincing than what you say

'Actions speak louder than words': what a person actually does matters more than what they say they will do. 'Don't just promise to help — actions speak louder than words.' One of the most common English proverbs, emphasizing the importance of deeds over promises.

Question 271medium

The word 'conundrum' means:

  1. A simple problem with an easy solution
  2. A confusing and difficult problem or question
  3. A type of puzzle game
  4. A formal announcement
Show answer

Answer: A confusing and difficult problem or question

Conundrum: a confusing and difficult problem or question; a riddle (especially one with a pun). Synonyms: puzzle, enigma, riddle, mystery, dilemma. 'The immigration policy presented a conundrum for policymakers.' Often used for problems that seem to have no clear solution.

Question 272medium

The word 'quandary' means:

  1. A type of mathematical problem
  2. A state of perplexity or uncertainty; a difficult situation
  3. Complete confidence in one's decision
  4. A type of geographical area
Show answer

Answer: A state of perplexity or uncertainty; a difficult situation

Quandary: a state of uncertainty or perplexity about what to do in a difficult situation; a dilemma. Synonyms: dilemma, predicament, difficulty, fix, bind. 'She was in a quandary about whether to accept the job offer.' Similar to 'conundrum' but emphasizes being stuck about what to do.

Question 273medium

Identify the correctly spelled word:

  1. Occurrance
  2. Occurance
  3. Occurrence
  4. Occurence
Show answer

Answer: Occurrence

'Occurrence' is the correct spelling: one 'c', then double 'r', then '-ence'. Memory trick: 'Occurrence has 2 c's and 2 r's — double trouble.' Common misspellings: 'occurrance', 'occurance', 'occurence'. Related: occur → occurring → occurrence.

Question 274hard

What type of word is 'however' in: 'The task was difficult. However, she completed it on time.'

  1. Coordinating conjunction
  2. Subordinating conjunction
  3. Conjunctive adverb
  4. Preposition
Show answer

Answer: Conjunctive adverb

'However' is a conjunctive adverb (also called transitional adverb). It connects ideas but is not a true conjunction. It shows contrast. Other conjunctive adverbs: therefore, moreover, nevertheless, consequently, furthermore, meanwhile, nonetheless. They need a semicolon before or a period (new sentence), not a comma alone.

Question 275medium

What is the grammatical term for 'run' in 'I go for a run every morning'?

  1. Verb
  2. Adjective
  3. Noun (used as a noun, not a verb)
  4. Adverb
Show answer

Answer: Noun (used as a noun, not a verb)

'Run' in 'a run every morning' functions as a noun (it is the object of the preposition 'for'). Many English words can function as different parts of speech depending on context. 'I run every morning' (verb), 'a run' (noun), 'running' (participle/gerund). Always identify part of speech by how the word functions in the sentence.

Question 276medium

The antonym of 'magnanimous' is:

  1. Generous
  2. Petty and mean-spirited
  3. Noble
  4. Forgiving
Show answer

Answer: Petty and mean-spirited

Magnanimous: very generous, especially toward a rival or enemy; big-hearted. Its antonym is 'petty' (small-minded, unforgiving) or 'mean-spirited' (unkind, malicious). Other antonyms: unforgiving, vengeful, small-minded, mean. Synonyms of magnanimous: gracious, noble, big-hearted.

Question 277medium

What does 'prima facie' mean?

  1. At the conclusion of an investigation
  2. Based on first impression; accepted as correct until proved otherwise
  3. After the fact
  4. Against all evidence
Show answer

Answer: Based on first impression; accepted as correct until proved otherwise

'Prima facie' (Latin: 'at first face/sight'): accepted as correct until proved otherwise; based on first impression. Common in law: 'a prima facie case' = case with sufficient evidence to go to trial unless rebutted. 'Prima facie evidence' = evidence that appears sufficient to establish a fact.

Question 278easy

Question 280medium

Harbinger: a person or thing that announces or signals the approach of another. 'The first signs of spring are a harbinger of warmer days.' Synonyms: omen, forerunner, precursor, portent, herald. 'Harbinger of doom' = something that signals disaster is coming.

Question 281easy

What is the 'rising action' in a story's plot structure?

  1. The initial background information
  2. A series of events that build tension and conflict, leading to the climax
  3. The resolution after the climax
  4. The turning point of the story
Show answer

Answer: A series of events that build tension and conflict, leading to the climax

Rising action: the series of events following the exposition that build up the conflict, tension, and suspense, leading toward the climax. Each event complicates the situation further. Plot structure: Exposition → Rising Action → Climax → Falling Action → Denouement.

Question 282medium

Choose the sentence with the correct punctuation of a quotation:

  1. She said, "I will be there soon".
  2. She said "I will be there soon."
  3. She said, "I will be there soon."
  4. She said: "I will be there soon",
Show answer

Answer: She said, "I will be there soon."

When a quotation follows a dialogue tag (she said, he replied), use a comma before the opening quotation mark. The period goes inside the closing quotation mark (American style): She said, "I will be there soon." British style places the period outside if not part of the original quotation.

Question 283hard

The literary technique 'in medias res' refers to:

  1. Starting a story from the very beginning
  2. Beginning a story in the middle of the action, without prior explanation
  3. Ending a story abruptly
  4. A flashback technique
Show answer

Answer: Beginning a story in the middle of the action, without prior explanation

'In medias res' (Latin: 'into the middle of things'): a narrative technique where the story begins in the middle of the action, with background filled in later through flashbacks or exposition. Used in Homer's Iliad and Odyssey, many modern films and novels.

Question 284medium

  3. Reticent
  4. Laconic
Show answer

Answer: Loquacious

'Loquacious' is a synonym for both 'verbose' (wordy) and 'garrulous' (excessively talkative). All three mean talking or writing too much. Synonyms: loquacious, verbose, garrulous, prolix, long-winded, rambling. Antonyms: taciturn, reticent, laconic, concise, brief.

Question 285easy

  3. To approve a decision

'Pass the buck': to shift responsibility or blame to someone else; to refuse to take responsibility for a problem. 'The manager kept passing the buck to his team instead of making decisions.' Popularized by President Harry Truman's desk sign: 'The buck stops here' (= I accept responsibility).

Question 286easy

Identify the figure of speech: 'The whole world is watching.'

  1. Metaphor
  2. Hyperbole (exaggeration — not literally every person)
  3. Personification
  4. Simile
Show answer

Answer: Hyperbole (exaggeration — not literally every person)

'The whole world is watching' = hyperbole (extreme exaggeration for emphasis; not literally every person on Earth is watching). Other hyperboles: 'I've told you a million times', 'I'm so hungry I could eat a horse', 'This bag weighs a ton'.

Question 287hard

What is 'zeugma' in grammar/rhetoric?

  1. Repetition of a word at the start of clauses
  2. A figure of speech where a word applies to two others in different senses
  3. Using contradictory terms
  4. An extended metaphor
Show answer

Answer: A figure of speech where a word applies to two others in different senses

Zeugma: a figure of speech where one word (usually a verb) applies to two others in different senses. Famous example: 'She opened the door and his heart.' ('opened' applies literally to 'door' and figuratively to 'heart'). Another: 'He lost his wallet and his temper.'

Question 288easy

What does 'to make ends meet' mean?

  2. To have just enough money to cover basic expenses
  3. To complete a long journey
  4. To resolve a conflict

'Make ends meet': to have just enough money to cover basic living expenses; to manage financially with difficulty. 'With rising prices, many families struggle to make ends meet.' Origin: balancing income and expenditure so both 'ends' of the budget meet.

Question 289easy

The word 'succinct' means:

  1. Very long and detailed
  2. Brief and clearly expressed; concise
  3. Confusing and hard to understand
  4. Very emotional
Show answer

Answer: Brief and clearly expressed; concise

Succinct: briefly and clearly expressed; saying much in few words. Synonyms: concise, brief, terse, laconic, compact. Antonyms: verbose, wordy, long-winded, prolix. 'A succinct summary captures the key points without unnecessary detail.'

Question 290medium

What is a 'mixed metaphor'?

  1. A metaphor that uses two different languages
  2. Combining two or more unrelated metaphors in a single expression, creating a confusing or absurd image
  3. A very powerful metaphor
  4. A metaphor used in scientific writing
Show answer

Answer: Combining two or more unrelated metaphors in a single expression, creating a confusing or absurd image

Mixed metaphor: combining two or more inconsistent metaphors in a way that creates an absurd or confused image. Example: 'We need to take the bull by the horns and nip this in the bud before it snowballs.' (Bull/horns, plant/bud, snowball = three unrelated images). Can accidentally create unintentional humor.

Question 291easy

What does the prefix 'poly-' mean in words like 'polygon', 'polyglot', 'polygamy'?

  1. One
  2. Two
  3. Many
  4. Half
Show answer

Answer: Many

'Poly-' (from Greek 'polys'): many. Polygon = many-sided figure. Polyglot = knows many languages. Polygamy = marriage to many spouses. Polyphony = many voices. Polymer = many units. Compare: mono- = one, bi-/di- = two, tri- = three, multi- = many (Latin).

Question 292medium

The word 'garrulous' is the opposite of which word?

  1. Loquacious
  2. Talkative
  3. Reticent
  4. Verbose
Show answer

Answer: Reticent

Garrulous = excessively talkative. Its opposite is 'reticent' = not revealing one's thoughts; quiet; reserved. Other antonyms: taciturn, laconic, uncommunicative. Synonyms of garrulous: loquacious, verbose, talkative, wordy, long-winded.

Question 293easy

What does 'per annum' mean?

  1. Per person
  2. Each year; annually
  3. Per item
  4. Each day
Show answer

Answer: Each year; annually

'Per annum' (Latin): each year; annually. 'Salary of ₹5 lakhs per annum' = salary of 5 lakhs each year. Used in financial, legal, and formal contexts. Similar: 'per capita' = per person; 'per diem' = per day; 'per se' = in itself.

Question 294easy

The word 'oblivious' means:

  1. Very alert and attentive

  3. Very forgetful of one's past
  4. Extremely organized
Show answer

Answer: Unaware of or not concerned about what is happening around you

Oblivious: not aware of or concerned about what is happening around one. 'She was oblivious to the chaos around her.' Synonyms: unaware, inattentive, heedless, ignorant, unmindful. Antonyms: aware, alert, attentive, mindful.

Question 295medium

Choose the sentence with the correct use of 'would rather':

  1. I would rather go to the beach than staying home.
  2. I would rather go to the beach than stay home.
  3. I would rather to go to the beach.
  4. I would rather going to the beach.
Show answer

Answer: I would rather go to the beach than stay home.

'Would rather + base verb...than + base verb': 'I would rather go...than stay.' The base verb (without 'to') is used after both 'would rather' and 'than'. Incorrect: 'would rather to go', 'would rather going', 'would rather...than staying'.

Question 296medium

The word 'ameliorate' is the antonym of:

  4. Achieve

Answer: Worsen / aggravate

Ameliorate means to make something better/improve. Its antonym is 'worsen' or 'aggravate' (to make worse). Synonyms of ameliorate: improve, better, enhance, alleviate. Antonyms: worsen, aggravate, exacerbate, deteriorate.

Question 297medium

Identify the error: 'He is one of the best student in the class.'

  1. 'one of' should be 'one among'
  2. 'student' should be 'students' — 'one of' is followed by a plural noun
  3. 'best' should be 'better'
  4. No error
Show answer

Answer: 'student' should be 'students' — 'one of' is followed by a plural noun

'One of the' must be followed by a plural noun: 'one of the best STUDENTS in the class.' 'One of the best student' is incorrect. Correct pattern: 'one of the + plural noun': 'one of the oldest cities', 'one of the finest examples', 'one of the best players'.

Incognito: having one's true identity concealed; in disguise or under an assumed name. 'The celebrity travelled incognito to avoid the paparazzi.' From Italian/Latin 'incognitus' = unknown. In technology: 'incognito mode' = private browsing where history is not recorded.

Question 299easy

'Turn a blind eye': to deliberately ignore something that you know is wrong or is happening. 'The authorities turned a blind eye to the illegal activity.' Origin: attributed to Admiral Horatio Nelson who literally put his telescope to his blind eye to avoid seeing a signal to retreat at the Battle of Copenhagen (1801).

Question 300medium

What is the literary device used in: 'The fog comes on little cat feet.' (Carl Sandburg)?

  2. Metaphor with personification — fog given a cat's characteristic movement
  3. Hyperbole
  4. Alliteration
Show answer

Answer: Metaphor with personification — fog given a cat's characteristic movement

This famous line uses metaphor (comparing fog to a cat, without 'like/as') and personification (giving the fog the catlike quality of moving silently and delicately on 'little cat feet'). From Carl Sandburg's poem 'Fog' (1916). The entire poem is an extended metaphor.

Question 301medium

  1. The lowest point
  2. The highest point; the peak or pinnacle of something
  3. A type of star
  4. The beginning of something
Show answer

Answer: The highest point; the peak or pinnacle of something

Zenith: the highest point in the sky directly above an observer; the peak or culmination of something. 'She reached the zenith of her career at 40.' Antonym: 'nadir' (the lowest point). Astronomical: the zenith is directly above you; the nadir is directly below.

Nadir: the lowest point; the worst or most difficult moment. 'The company's nadir was when it declared bankruptcy.' Antonym: 'zenith' (the highest point). Astronomical meaning: the point on the celestial sphere directly below the observer (opposite of zenith).

Question 304medium

What is a 'foil' character in literature?

  1. A main character who changes dramatically
  2. A character who contrasts with another character (especially the protagonist) to highlight certain qualities
  3. A minor character with no importance
  4. The narrator of the story
Show answer

Answer: A character who contrasts with another character (especially the protagonist) to highlight certain qualities

Foil: a character who contrasts with another (often the protagonist) to highlight particular qualities. The contrast makes both characters' traits more apparent. Classic examples: Dr. Watson foils Sherlock Holmes (Watson = ordinary; Holmes = brilliant); Laertes foils Hamlet (Laertes = decisive; Hamlet = indecisive).

'In the nick of time': just in time; at the very last possible moment before it's too late. 'The firefighters arrived in the nick of time to save the building.' Synonyms: just in time, at the last minute, barely in time. 'Nick' here means a precise point or notch in time.

Question 306easy

  3. Every month
  4. During special occasions only
Show answer

Answer: Very rarely; on very few occasions

'Once in a blue moon': very rarely; on very infrequent occasions. 'She only visits once in a blue moon.' A 'blue moon' is the second full moon in a calendar month, which happens about once every 2.5 years — hence, very rarely. Synonyms: rarely, seldom, infrequently, almost never.

Question 310medium

What is the meaning of 'catch-22'?

  1. A rule in the military
  2. A situation where a desired outcome is impossible because of a contradictory rule or condition
  3. A type of agreement
  4. A second chance at something
Show answer

Answer: A situation where a desired outcome is impossible because of a contradictory rule or condition

'Catch-22': a dilemma or impossible situation where you are trapped by a set of paradoxical conditions — any attempt to solve one problem creates another. From Joseph Heller's novel 'Catch-22' (1961). Classic example: you need experience to get a job, but you need a job to get experience.

Question 311easy

'The ball is in your court': it is your turn to take action or make the next move; the decision or responsibility now lies with you. 'I've done my part; the ball is in your court.' From tennis, where when the ball is in your court, you must play it.

Question 312easy

The word 'accolade' means:

  3. A formal complaint
  4. A type of decoration

Accolade: an award or expression of praise, approval, or admiration. 'The film received many accolades at the festival.' Synonyms: award, honour, praise, recognition, commendation. Antonyms: criticism, condemnation. Originally, an accolade was the gesture of conferring knighthood (a touch on the shoulder with a sword).

Question 313hard

What is 'transferred epithet' (hypallage) in grammar?

  1. An epithet that describes a different noun than the one it grammatically modifies
  2. A type of metaphor
  3. An adjective borrowed from another language
  4. A misplaced adjective due to a grammar error
Show answer

Answer: An epithet that describes a different noun than the one it grammatically modifies

Transferred epithet (hypallage): an adjective logically describing one thing is transferred to another. 'She spent a sleepless night' — technically, the night is not sleepless, SHE is sleepless. Other examples: 'a restless journey', 'an anxious hour', 'a miserable day'. Common in poetry and literature.

Question 314medium

Choose the correct form: 'She is used to ___ late.'

  1. work
  2. worked
  3. working
  4. being work
Show answer

Answer: working

'Be used to' + gerund (verb+-ing): 'She is used to working late.' 'Be used to' means to be accustomed to; it is followed by a gerund (noun form). Contrast: 'used to + base verb' (past habit): 'She used to work late.' These two constructions have completely different meanings.

Question 315medium

Identify the figure of speech: 'The pen is mightier than the sword.'

  1. Simile
  2. Metaphor with metonymy — pen represents writing; sword represents violence
  3. Hyperbole
  4. Alliteration
Show answer

Answer: Metaphor with metonymy — pen represents writing; sword represents violence

'The pen is mightier than the sword' uses: (1) Metaphor: pen and sword used symbolically; (2) Metonymy: 'pen' represents writing/ideas; 'sword' represents military force/violence. The sentence makes a direct comparison (metaphor) using these substitute terms (metonymy). Coined by Edward Bulwer-Lytton (1839).

Question 316medium

What is the correct plural of 'appendix' in academic writing?

  1. Appendixes only
  2. Appendices (formal/academic) or Appendixes (general)
  3. Appendici
  4. Appendixs
Show answer

Answer: Appendices (formal/academic) or Appendixes (general)

'Appendix' has two accepted plurals: 'appendices' (formal, academic, medical) and 'appendixes' (general usage). In academic books: 'See Appendices A and B.' In medicine: surgical removal = appendectomy (referring to the vermiform appendix; its plural: appendices). Both are correct.

Question 317easy

What is the difference between a 'simile' and a 'metaphor'?

  1. They are exactly the same figure of speech
  2. A simile uses 'like' or 'as' for comparison; a metaphor makes a direct comparison without 'like' or 'as'
  3. A metaphor is used in poetry only; a simile in prose
  4. A simile compares people; a metaphor compares objects
Show answer

Answer: A simile uses 'like' or 'as' for comparison; a metaphor makes a direct comparison without 'like' or 'as'

Simile: uses 'like' or 'as': 'She is LIKE a ray of sunshine.' 'He was AS brave AS a lion.' Metaphor: direct comparison without 'like' or 'as': 'She IS a ray of sunshine.' 'He IS a lion in battle.' Both are figures of speech comparing two unlike things. Metaphors are typically stronger and more direct.

Question 318easy

What is the meaning of the Latin phrase 'veni, vidi, vici'?

  1. I came, I saw, I conquered (Julius Caesar)
  2. I think, therefore I am (Descartes)
  3. In the beginning was the word
  4. To be or not to be (Shakespeare)
Show answer

Answer: I came, I saw, I conquered (Julius Caesar)

'Veni, vidi, vici' (Latin): 'I came, I saw, I conquered.' Julius Caesar used this phrase in a report to Rome after a swift military victory at the Battle of Zela (47 BC). It has become one of the most famous Latin phrases, symbolizing swift, decisive victory. Its brevity reflects Caesar's style.

Question 319medium

What is the one-word substitute for 'study of handwriting to assess personality'?

  1. Calligraphy
  2. Graphology
  3. Typography
  4. Chirography
Show answer

Answer: Graphology

Graphology: the study of handwriting, especially as used to infer a person's character. From Greek 'grapho' (write) + 'logos' (study). Calligraphy: the art of beautiful writing. Typography: the art of arranging type/fonts. Chirography: handwriting or penmanship (more technical term).

Question 320medium

'The deafening silence' is an example of:

  2. Alliteration
  3. Oxymoron (contradictory words combined)
  4. Metaphor
Show answer

Answer: Oxymoron (contradictory words combined)

An oxymoron combines contradictory or opposite ideas in one phrase: 'deafening silence' (deafening = very loud; silence = absence of sound). Other examples: 'sweet sorrow', 'living dead', 'open secret'.

'A lot of' takes a singular OR plural verb depending on the noun that follows. 'Money' is uncountable (singular) → 'is needed'. 'A lot of books are...' (countable plural → are).

Question 325easy

The main idea is the central thought or topic of a passage that all other details support. It is often stated in the topic sentence (usually first or last sentence of a paragraph).

Question 326easy

Inference: reading between the lines — using evidence from the text and prior knowledge to draw conclusions about information NOT explicitly stated. A key reading skill.

Question 327medium

Arrange the sentences: P: She loves mangoes. Q: India is a tropical country. R: Mangoes grow well in tropical climates. S: Riya lives in India.

  1. QRSP
  2. QRPS
  3. SRPQ
  4. PSQR
Show answer

Answer: QRSP

Logical order: Q (India is tropical) → R (mangoes grow in tropical climates) → S (Riya lives in India) → P (she loves mangoes). This makes a coherent paragraph.

Question 328medium

Which sentence shows correct use of 'affect' vs 'effect'?

  1. The medicine will effect the patient badly.
  2. The medicine will affect the patient badly.
  3. The affect of the medicine was good.
  4. The medicine had a good affect.
Show answer

Answer: The medicine will affect the patient badly.

'Affect' is usually a VERB (to influence). 'Effect' is usually a NOUN (result/outcome). 'The medicine will AFFECT the patient' (verb). 'The EFFECT of the medicine was good' (noun).

Question 337hard

Which sentence has the correct use of 'who' vs 'whom'?

  1. Who did you invite to the party?
  2. Whom called you last night?
  3. Whom I spoke to was very helpful.
  4. To whom should I address the letter?
Show answer

Answer: To whom should I address the letter?

'Who' is a subject pronoun; 'whom' is an object pronoun. Use 'whom' when it is the object of a verb or preposition. 'To whom should I address the letter?' — 'whom' is object of 'to'. Test: replace with 'he/she' (subject→who) or 'him/her' (object→whom). 'I spoke to him' → 'whom I spoke to'.

Question 338medium

Select the sentence free of error:

  1. The committee have decided to postpone the meeting.
  2. The committee has decided to postpone the meeting.
  3. The committee had been decided to postpone the meeting.
  4. The committee are deciding to postpone the meeting yesterday.
Show answer

Answer: The committee has decided to postpone the meeting.

Collective nouns (committee, team, jury, family, class, government) take singular verbs in American English when acting as a unit: 'The committee has decided.' In British English, plural is sometimes acceptable, but for TNPSC/SSC, treat collective nouns as singular.

Question 339easy

What is a 'cloze test' in English grammar?

  1. A test of listening comprehension
  2. A test where words are removed from a passage and must be filled in from context
  3. A test of speaking skills
  4. A multiple-choice vocabulary test
Show answer

Answer: A test where words are removed from a passage and must be filled in from context

A cloze test (from 'closure') is a fill-in-the-blank exercise where words are systematically removed from a passage. Test-takers must supply appropriate words using context clues. Tests reading comprehension, vocabulary, and grammar simultaneously. Common in TNPSC, SSC, UPSC exams.

Question 340medium

Identify the word that is spelled incorrectly:

  1. Exaggerate
  2. Millennium
  3. Parallel
  4. Begining
Show answer

Answer: Begining

'Beginning' is spelled with double 'n' and double 'n': 'beginning' (begin + n + ing, consonant doubled before suffix when the syllable is stressed). Correct spellings: exaggerate (2 g's), millennium (2 l's + 2 n's), parallel (2 l's in middle).

Question 341easy

In the sentence 'The tall, dark, handsome man walked in', the words 'tall, dark, handsome' are:

  1. Adverbs modifying the verb 'walked'
  2. Adjectives modifying the noun 'man'
  3. Pronouns replacing 'man'
  4. Conjunctions joining clauses
Show answer

Answer: Adjectives modifying the noun 'man'

Adjectives describe or modify nouns/pronouns. 'Tall, dark, handsome' describe the noun 'man'. When multiple adjectives appear before a noun, they are usually separated by commas and follow OSACOMP order: Opinion, Size, Age, Color, Origin, Material, Purpose.

Question 342medium

Which sentence demonstrates correct use of 'although' vs 'despite'?

  1. Although the rain, we continued playing.
  2. Despite it rained, we continued playing.
  3. Although it rained, we continued playing.
  4. Despite of the rain, we continued playing.
Show answer

Answer: Although it rained, we continued playing.

'Although/though/even though' introduce a clause (subject + verb): 'Although it rained...' 'Despite/in spite of' introduce a noun or gerund: 'Despite the rain...' or 'Despite raining...'. NOT 'despite of' — that is incorrect. 'Despite of' is a common error.

Question 343medium

Which sentence has an error in tense?

  1. When I arrived, she had already left.
  2. I was watching TV when the phone rang.
  3. By next year, I will have completed my studies.
  4. She has visited Paris last year.
Show answer

Answer: She has visited Paris last year.

'Last year' is a specific past time, so past simple must be used: 'She visited Paris last year.' Present perfect ('has visited') cannot be used with specific past time expressions like yesterday, last year, in 2020, ago. These require simple past.

Obsolete: no longer produced or used; out of date. Synonyms: outdated, archaic, antiquated, defunct, superseded. Antonyms: current, modern, new, up-to-date. E.g., 'The fax machine has become obsolete in the age of email.'

Question 347easy
