Question 348easy

A 'biography' is:

  1. An autobiography written by the person themselves
  2. A life story written by someone else about another person
  3. A fictional story about a person's life
  4. A collection of poems about a person
Show answer

Answer: A life story written by someone else about another person

Biography: the story of someone's life written by another person. Auto-biography: the story of one's own life written by themselves. Memoir: a personal account of specific experiences. Bibliography: a list of sources/books used in writing.

Question 349hard

The literary device where a part represents the whole (or vice versa) is called:

  1. Metaphor
  2. Synecdoche
  3. Irony
  4. Allegory
Show answer

Answer: Synecdoche

Synecdoche: a figure of speech where a part is used to represent the whole or vice versa. Examples: 'All hands on deck' (hands = sailors), 'hired a few heads of cattle' (heads = cattle), 'wheels' (= car), 'the pen is mightier than the sword' (pen = writing/words).

Question 350medium

The use of 'irony' involves:

  1. Saying exactly what you mean
  2. Saying the opposite of what you mean to create effect, or a situation opposite to what is expected
  3. Using very old language
  4. Comparing two unlike things
Show answer

Answer: Saying the opposite of what you mean to create effect, or a situation opposite to what is expected

Irony: saying the opposite of what is meant (verbal irony), or a situation where the outcome is opposite to what was expected (situational irony). Example: Saying 'What lovely weather!' during a storm. Dramatic irony: the audience knows something characters don't.

Question 351easy

Choose the correctly formed sentence using 'since' or 'for':

  1. I have been waiting since two hours.
  2. I have been waiting for two hours.
  3. I have been waiting since two hours ago.
  4. I am waiting since two hours.
Show answer

Answer: I have been waiting for two hours.

'For' is used with a duration (period of time): for two hours, for three days, for a year. 'Since' is used with a point in time: since 2 o'clock, since Monday, since 1990, since last year. 'I have been waiting for two hours.'

Question 352easy

What does 'the best of both worlds' mean?

  1. Living in two countries
  2. Enjoying advantages of two different options simultaneously
  3. Making a difficult choice between two things
  4. Being unable to decide between two options
Show answer

Answer: Enjoying advantages of two different options simultaneously

'The best of both worlds' means enjoying the advantages of two different things simultaneously, without the disadvantages of either. E.g., 'Working from home gives me the best of both worlds — professional work and family time.'

Question 353medium

In formal letter writing, which of the following is the correct complimentary close for a letter to an unknown person?

  1. Yours lovingly
  2. Yours affectionately
  3. Yours faithfully
  4. Yours sincerely
Show answer

Answer: Yours faithfully

'Yours faithfully' is used when the letter begins with 'Dear Sir/Madam' (addressee unknown). 'Yours sincerely' is used when the letter begins with the person's name (e.g., 'Dear Mr. Sharma'). 'Yours lovingly/affectionately' are used in personal/informal letters.

Question 354easy

Identify the correct preposition: 'She has been living in Chennai ___ 2019.'

  1. for
  2. since
  3. during
  4. from
Show answer

Answer: since

'Since' is used with a specific point in time (2019 = a year). 'For' is used with duration ('for five years'). 'Since 2019' tells us when the action started and implies it is still continuing. Present perfect or present perfect continuous is typically used with 'since'.

Question 355medium

What is 'euphemism'?

  1. An aggressive or harsh expression
  2. A mild or indirect expression substituted for a harsh or unpleasant one
  3. A comparison using 'like' or 'as'
  4. Repetition of words for emphasis
Show answer

Answer: A mild or indirect expression substituted for a harsh or unpleasant one

Euphemism: a polite or indirect expression used instead of a blunt, harsh, or unpleasant one. Examples: 'passed away' (died), 'between jobs' (unemployed), 'economical with the truth' (lying), 'pre-owned' (used). Opposite: dysphemism (intentionally harsh expression).

Question 356hard

Select the sentence with correct subject-verb agreement:

  1. Mathematics are my favourite subject.
  2. The news are shocking.
  3. Neither of the two candidates are qualified.
  4. Neither of the two candidates is qualified.
Show answer

Answer: Neither of the two candidates is qualified.

'Neither' (used alone) takes a singular verb: 'Neither is qualified.' After 'neither of the', the noun is plural but the verb should still be singular: 'Neither of the candidates is...' Similarly: 'each of them is', 'none of them is' (formally), 'everyone was'.

Question 357easy

What does the prefix 'mis-' mean in words like 'misunderstand', 'misconduct', 'misplace'?

  1. Good/well
  2. Wrongly or badly
  3. Again
  4. Before
Show answer

Answer: Wrongly or badly

Prefix 'mis-' means wrongly or badly: misunderstand (understand wrongly), misconduct (bad conduct), misplace (place wrongly), mislead (lead wrongly), misspell (spell wrongly). Other prefixes: 're-' = again; 'pre-' = before; 'anti-' = against; 'un-'/'dis-' = not.

Question 358easy

What does the suffix '-ology' mean?

  1. Fear of something
  2. Study of/science of something
  3. Relating to
  4. Without
Show answer

Answer: Study of/science of something

Suffix '-ology' means the study of or science of: biology (study of life), geology (study of earth), psychology (study of mind), sociology (study of society), dermatology (study of skin). 'Phobia' = fear of; '-itis' = inflammation of; '-ectomy' = surgical removal of.

Question 359medium

The word 'ambivalent' means:

  1. Strongly in favour of something
  2. Having mixed feelings or contradictory ideas about something
  3. Strongly against something
  4. Completely indifferent
Show answer

Answer: Having mixed feelings or contradictory ideas about something

Ambivalent: having mixed or contradictory feelings about something or someone simultaneously. Example: 'She felt ambivalent about moving abroad — excited about the opportunity but sad to leave family.' Synonyms: undecided, uncertain, torn, equivocal.

Question 360medium

In which sentence is the word 'since' correctly used?

  1. I haven't seen him since a long time.
  2. I haven't seen him since last year.
  3. I haven't seen him since many years.
  4. I haven't seen him since three weeks.
Show answer

Answer: I haven't seen him since last year.

'Since' is followed by a specific point in time, not a duration: 'since last year', 'since Monday', 'since 8 o'clock', 'since childhood'. 'For' is used with durations: 'for many years', 'for a long time', 'for three weeks'. WRONG: 'since three weeks' — use 'for three weeks'.

Question 361hard

The literary term for the reversal of the expected order of words in a sentence for emphasis is called:

  1. Hyperbole
  2. Inversion
  3. Litotes
  4. Zeugma
Show answer

Answer: Inversion

Inversion (anastrophe): reversing the normal word order for emphasis or poetic effect. Examples: 'Into the room walked the king' (normal: 'The king walked into the room'), 'Blessed are the meek' (normal: 'The meek are blessed'), 'Never have I seen such beauty'.

Question 362medium

What does the phrase 'per se' mean?

  1. For example
  2. In itself; by itself; intrinsically
  3. On the other hand
  4. Without doubt
Show answer

Answer: In itself; by itself; intrinsically

'Per se' (Latin): by or in itself; intrinsically. 'The job title per se doesn't matter; what matters is the work.' It means the thing being discussed, taken by itself and not in relation to other things. Common in formal/legal usage.

Question 363easy

What does the phrase 'status quo' mean?

  1. The highest status
  2. The existing state of affairs
  3. A high rank or position
  4. The final outcome
Show answer

Answer: The existing state of affairs

'Status quo' (Latin): the current or existing state of affairs; the way things are now. 'They want to maintain the status quo' = they don't want things to change. Common in politics and social discussion.

Question 364medium

Choose the sentence that correctly uses 'number' vs 'amount':

  1. A large amount of students attended the lecture.
  2. A large number of students attended the lecture.
  3. A large number of water was wasted.
  4. A large amount of trees were cut down.
Show answer

Answer: A large number of students attended the lecture.

'Number' is used for countable nouns: 'a number of students, a number of cars, a number of trees.' 'Amount' is used for uncountable nouns: 'a large amount of water, an amount of money, an amount of time.' Common error: 'amount of people' (should be 'number of people').

Question 365medium

The word 'altruistic' means:

  1. Selfish and self-centered
  2. Showing selfless concern for the well-being of others
  3. Aggressive and competitive
  4. Indifferent to others
Show answer

Answer: Showing selfless concern for the well-being of others

Altruistic: having or showing concern for others' well-being rather than one's own; selfless. Synonyms: selfless, philanthropic, charitable, benevolent. Antonyms: selfish, self-centered, egotistical. Coined by Auguste Comte from Italian 'altrui' = others.

Question 366medium

Which pair are homophones?

  1. Accept / Except
  2. Their / There
  3. Affect / Effect
  4. Principal / Principle
Show answer

Answer: Their / There

Homophones are words that sound the same but have different meanings and spellings. 'Their' and 'there' sound identical but have different meanings. Accept/Except, Affect/Effect, Principal/Principle — these are commonly confused but are NOT homophones (they sound different).

Question 367easy

Which is the correctly spelled word?

  1. Occassion
  2. Occassion
  3. Ocassion
  4. Occasion
Show answer

Answer: Occasion

Correct spelling: 'Occasion' (1 c + 2 s). Memory trick: 'On occasion, I see 1 cat and 2 snakes' (1 c + 2 s). Other commonly misspelled words: possession (1 p + 2 s + 2 s), profession (2 s), occasion (1 c + 2 s).

Question 368medium

The difference between 'further' and 'farther' is:

  1. They are completely interchangeable
  2. 'Farther' refers to physical distance; 'further' refers to figurative or additional extent/degree
  3. 'Further' is British English; 'farther' is American English
  4. 'Further' refers to distance; 'farther' means additional
Show answer

Answer: 'Farther' refers to physical distance; 'further' refers to figurative or additional extent/degree

Traditional distinction: 'Farther' = physical distance: 'The school is farther away.' 'Further' = additional degree, amount, or in figurative sense: 'We need no further discussion.' In modern usage, 'further' is increasingly used for both, but 'farther' is strictly for physical distance.

Question 369medium

Which sentence correctly uses 'who' vs 'that'?

  1. The dog that I saw was very friendly.
  2. The student that passed the exam celebrated.
  3. The teacher who inspired me most retired last year.
  4. The company that he works is very large.
Show answer

Answer: The teacher who inspired me most retired last year.

'Who' is used for people; 'that' is used for things and animals (informally, also for people in defining clauses). 'The teacher who inspired me' = correct. 'The dog that I saw' = also acceptable. But for specific identification of people in formal writing, 'who' is preferred.

Question 370medium

Choose the sentence with the correct use of 'principal' vs 'principle':

  1. The principle reason for the delay was the rain.
  2. She stood by her principals in the face of pressure.
  3. The principal reason for the delay was the rain.
  4. He was a man of strong principals.
Show answer

Answer: The principal reason for the delay was the rain.

'Principal' (adjective): main, primary, chief. 'Principal' (noun): head of a school or main person. 'Principle' (noun): a fundamental rule or belief. 'The principal reason' = the main reason (adjective). 'He is a man of strong principles' = values/beliefs.

Question 371easy

Choose the sentence with the correct use of 'accept' vs 'except':

  1. I accept all the invitations except the one on Monday.
  2. I except all the invitations accept the one on Monday.
  3. I accept all invitations accept the one on Monday.
  4. I except all invitations except the one on Monday.
Show answer

Answer: I accept all the invitations except the one on Monday.

'Accept' (verb): to receive or agree to something. 'Except' (preposition/conjunction): excluding. 'I accept all the invitations except the one on Monday.' = I agree to all invitations, excluding Monday's. These are often confused in writing.

Question 372medium

The term 'oxymoron' describes:

  1. An extended metaphor throughout a work
  2. A combination of contradictory terms
  3. Exaggeration for effect
  4. Using the same consonant sounds
  5. Giving human attributes to objects
Show answer

Answer: A combination of contradictory terms

Oxymoron: a figure of speech combining two seemingly contradictory terms for effect. Examples: deafening silence, living death, sweet sorrow, bittersweet, open secret, clearly confused, alone together, original copy, act naturally, only choice.

Question 373medium

The literary term 'foreshadowing' means:

  1. Looking back at past events
  2. Providing clues about events that will occur later in the story
  3. Describing a character's inner thoughts
  4. Interrupting the chronological order of events
Show answer

Answer: Providing clues about events that will occur later in the story

Foreshadowing: a literary technique where the author hints at or signals future events. It creates suspense and prepares the reader. Examples: a dark storm before a tragedy, a character finding a weapon before a fight, an ominous statement. Opposite: flashback (looking back at the past).

Question 374medium

What is a 'paradox' in literature?

  1. A comparison using 'like' or 'as'
  2. A statement that appears contradictory but may contain a deeper truth
  3. A type of poem without rhyme
  4. An extended metaphor
Show answer

Answer: A statement that appears contradictory but may contain a deeper truth

Paradox: a seemingly contradictory statement that may nonetheless be true. Examples: 'Less is more', 'I must be cruel to be kind', 'The only way to lose weight is to eat more' (in certain contexts). Famous paradox: 'I know that I know nothing' (Socrates).

Question 375hard

Which sentence has a misplaced modifier?

  1. She bought a dress from the shop made of silk.
  2. She bought a silk dress from the shop.
  3. The shop sold a silk dress to her.
  4. She bought a dress made of silk from the shop.
Show answer

Answer: She bought a dress from the shop made of silk.

Misplaced modifier: a modifier placed too far from the word it modifies, creating confusion. 'A dress from the shop made of silk' — 'made of silk' appears to modify 'the shop'. Correct: 'She bought a silk dress from the shop' or 'She bought a dress made of silk from the shop.'

Question 376medium

The phrase 'cold turkey' means:

  1. A festive dish
  2. Suddenly stopping a habit without gradual reduction
  3. Being cold and unfriendly
  4. A type of weather
Show answer

Answer: Suddenly stopping a habit without gradual reduction

'Cold turkey': to suddenly and completely stop doing something addictive (like smoking or a drug habit) without gradual weaning. 'She quit smoking cold turkey.' Also used more broadly for abrupt stopping of any activity. Origin: the pallor/goosebumps of a withdrawing addict resembling a plucked cold turkey.

Question 377medium

What does 'ad hoc' mean?

  1. According to the law
  2. Created or done for a particular purpose as necessary, not planned in advance
  3. Against the norm
  4. Over and above
Show answer

Answer: Created or done for a particular purpose as necessary, not planned in advance

'Ad hoc' (Latin: 'for this'): created or done for a particular purpose as necessary; not planned or systematic. 'An ad hoc committee was formed to deal with the emergency.' Contrasted with 'standing committee' (permanent). Common in business, government, legal contexts.

Question 378medium

Which sentence correctly uses 'emigrate' vs 'immigrate'?

  1. She immigrated from India to Canada.
  2. She emigrated from India to Canada.
  3. She emigrated to India from Canada.
  4. She immigrated from Canada, leaving India.
Show answer

Answer: She emigrated from India to Canada.

Emigrate: to leave your own country to live in another country (focus on departure). Immigrate: to come into another country to live permanently (focus on arrival). 'She emigrated from India' = left India. 'She immigrated to Canada' = arrived in Canada. Same event, different focus.

Question 379easy

What does the idiomatic expression 'to see eye to eye' mean?

  1. To look at each other
  2. To agree with someone
  3. To watch something carefully
  4. To examine something closely
Show answer

Answer: To agree with someone

'To see eye to eye' means to agree with someone; to share the same viewpoint or opinion. 'We don't always see eye to eye on politics, but we respect each other.' Opposite expressions: 'to disagree', 'to be at odds', 'to clash'.

Question 380easy

What does the expression 'on the fence' mean?

  1. Sitting on a wall
  2. Being undecided or neutral about an issue
  3. Supporting one side strongly
  4. Being involved in outdoor activities
Show answer

Answer: Being undecided or neutral about an issue

'On the fence': undecided or neutral about an issue, not committing to either side. 'She is still on the fence about whether to accept the job offer.' Synonyms: undecided, ambivalent, noncommittal, hesitant. To 'sit on the fence' = refuse to take a side.

Question 381medium

The word 'redundant' in language means:

  1. Very necessary and important
  2. Unnecessary because it repeats something already stated; superfluous
  3. Rare and uncommon
  4. Very complex and difficult
Show answer

Answer: Unnecessary because it repeats something already stated; superfluous

Redundant: using more words than necessary; repeating something already stated. Examples of redundant phrases: 'free gift' (a gift is always free), 'past history' (history is always past), 'advance planning', 'end result', 'unexpected surprise'. Strunk & White: 'Omit needless words.'

Question 382easy

What is the meaning of the phrase 'the ball is in your court'?

  1. It is time to play sports
  2. It is your turn to take action or make a decision
  3. The situation is out of control
  4. You have won the argument
Show answer

Answer: It is your turn to take action or make a decision

'The ball is in your court': it is now up to you to act or make the next move; the decision or responsibility is yours. From tennis, where when the ball is in your court, it's your turn to play. 'I've made my offer; now the ball is in your court.'

Question 383hard

Select the sentence with the correct word order for an indirect question:

  1. She asked me where was he going.
  2. She asked me where he was going.
  3. She asked me where is he going.
  4. She asked where was going he.
Show answer

Answer: She asked me where he was going.

In indirect (reported) questions, the word order follows statement order (subject + verb), NOT question order (verb + subject). 'Where was he going?' (direct question) → 'She asked me where he was going' (indirect). Do NOT use question word order in indirect questions.

Question 384easy

What type of noun is 'flock' in 'a flock of birds'?

  1. Proper noun
  2. Abstract noun
  3. Collective noun
  4. Compound noun
Show answer

Answer: Collective noun

Collective noun: a word denoting a group of people/animals/things. 'A flock of birds'. Other collective nouns: a herd of cattle, a pride of lions, a school of fish, a pack of wolves, a murder of crows, a gaggle of geese, a parliament of owls, a colony of ants.

What is the collective noun for 'fish'?

  1. Pack
  2. School (or shoal)
  3. Herd

Answer: School (or shoal)

A school (or shoal) of fish. Other animal group nouns: pride of lions, murder of crows, gaggle of geese, parliament of owls, pod of whales/dolphins, colony of ants/bats, pack of wolves/dogs, army of ants, flamboyance of flamingos, crash of rhinoceroses.

Question 386medium

What does 'lingua franca' mean?

  1. The French language only
  2. A language used as a common means of communication between speakers of different languages
  3. A dialect of a language
  4. An ancient and dead language
Show answer

Answer: A language used as a common means of communication between speakers of different languages

Lingua franca: a language adopted as a common language between speakers whose native languages are different. English is the current global lingua franca. Historically, Latin, French, and Swahili (East Africa) have served this role. From Italian 'Frankish tongue' — medieval trade language of the Mediterranean.

Question 387medium

The antonym of 'explicit' is:

  1. Exact
  2. Implied or not clearly expressed; implicit
  3. Very detailed
  4. Certain and clear
Show answer

Answer: Implied or not clearly expressed; implicit

Explicit: stated clearly, leaving nothing implied; direct. Implicit: implied or suggested but not directly stated. Explicit = expressed. Implicit = implied. 'Explicit instructions' leave no doubt; 'implicit understanding' is something understood without being said.

Question 388hard

Which sentence correctly uses a 'participial phrase' as a modifier?

  1. Running quickly, the bus was missed by him.
  2. Running quickly, he caught the bus.
  3. He running quickly, caught the bus.
  4. Running quickly he catches the bus incorrectly.
Show answer

Answer: Running quickly, he caught the bus.

A participial phrase modifies the subject that immediately follows it. 'Running quickly, he caught the bus' — 'he' was running (correct). 'Running quickly, the bus was missed' = wrong (the bus wasn't running). The participial phrase must refer to the subject of the main clause to avoid a dangling modifier.

Question 389easy

What does 'to go back to the drawing board' mean?

  1. To practice drawing
  2. To start something from the beginning after a failure
  3. To review financial plans
  4. To revisit a museum
Show answer

Answer: To start something from the beginning after a failure

'Back to the drawing board' = to start something from the very beginning because the previous attempt failed. 'Our plan didn't work, so it's back to the drawing board.' Origin: when engineering designs failed, designers literally went back to their drawing boards to start new designs.

Question 390easy

What does 'to miss the boat' mean?

  1. To arrive at the harbour late
  2. To fail to take advantage of an opportunity
  3. To make an error in navigation
  4. To lose an important item
Show answer

Answer: To fail to take advantage of an opportunity

'Miss the boat' = to fail to take advantage of an opportunity because you waited too long or weren't ready. 'He missed the boat by not investing in that company early on.' Similar: 'miss the train', 'the ship has sailed'. Opposite: 'seize the opportunity'.

Question 391medium

The word 'garrulous' means:

  1. Very quiet and reserved
  2. Excessively talkative, especially about trivial things
  3. Very angry and aggressive
  4. Sad and melancholy
Show answer

Answer: Excessively talkative, especially about trivial things

Garrulous: excessively talkative, especially about trivial matters. Synonyms: talkative, loquacious, verbose, chatty, long-winded. Antonyms: taciturn, reticent, quiet, reserved. 'A garrulous neighbor' never stops talking about unimportant things.

Question 392easy

What is an 'acronym'?

  1. A word that reads the same backwards
  2. A word formed from the initial letters of other words (pronounced as a word)
  3. A word that sounds like another word
  4. A very long compound word
Show answer

Answer: A word formed from the initial letters of other words (pronounced as a word)

Acronym: a word formed from the first letters of each word in a phrase, pronounced as a word. Examples: NASA (National Aeronautics and Space Administration), LASER (Light Amplification by Stimulated Emission of Radiation), RADAR, AIDS, SCUBA. Distinct from initialism (e.g., BBC, USA — spelled out letter by letter).

Question 393hard

In grammar, 'ellipsis' refers to:

  1. A very long sentence
  2. The omission of words that are understood from context
  3. A punctuation mark (...)
  4. A type of rhetorical question
Show answer

Answer: The omission of words that are understood from context

Ellipsis (grammatical): the omission of words that would repeat or be clearly understood from context. Example: 'She likes coffee and he [likes] tea' — 'likes' is omitted. Also refers to the three dots (...) indicating something is left unsaid or a pause/omission in quoted text.

Question 394easy

Choose the sentence with correct punctuation:

  1. Its a beautiful day today.
  2. It's a beautiful day today.
  3. Its' a beautiful day today.
  4. Its is a beautiful day today.
Show answer

Answer: It's a beautiful day today.

'It's' (with apostrophe) = 'it is' or 'it has' (contraction). 'Its' (without apostrophe) = possessive pronoun ('The dog wagged its tail'). 'It's a beautiful day' = 'It is a beautiful day'. Common confusion: apostrophes in possessive pronouns (its, yours, hers, theirs) are NEVER used.

Question 395medium

What is a 'subordinating conjunction'?

  1. A word that joins two independent clauses of equal importance
  2. A word that introduces a dependent clause and shows its relationship to the main clause
  3. A conjunction that connects two words of the same grammatical type
  4. A word used to replace a noun
Show answer

Answer: A word that introduces a dependent clause and shows its relationship to the main clause

Subordinating conjunctions introduce dependent/subordinate clauses: because, although, when, since, if, unless, while, after, before, until, as, though, whether, wherever. They show relationships like cause (because), condition (if), contrast (although), time (when). They make one clause dependent on another.

Question 396medium

Which coordinating conjunction from FANBOYS shows contrast?

  1. And
  2. But
  3. Or
  4. So
Show answer

Answer: But

FANBOYS coordinating conjunctions: For (reason), And (addition), Nor (neither), But (contrast), Or (alternative), Yet (contrast), So (result). 'But' shows contrast: 'She tried hard, but she failed.' 'Yet' also shows contrast: 'He is poor, yet he is happy.'

Question 397medium

Select the correct passive voice transformation: 'Someone stole his bicycle.'

  1. His bicycle was stolen.
  2. His bicycle is stolen.
  3. His bicycle has stolen.
  4. His bicycle was being stolen.
Show answer

Answer: His bicycle was stolen.

Active: 'Someone stole his bicycle' (past simple). Passive transformation: subject → passive subject; verb → was/were + past participle; agent ('someone' = vague) → omitted. 'His bicycle was stolen.' The vague subject 'someone' is dropped. Past simple active → past simple passive (was/were + past participle).

Question 398easy

What does 'the tip of the iceberg' mean?

  1. A small problem in the Arctic
  2. A small visible part of a much larger hidden problem or situation
  3. An obvious and easy-to-solve issue
  4. The most important part of any problem
Show answer

Answer: A small visible part of a much larger hidden problem or situation

'The tip of the iceberg': the small visible part of a much larger problem or situation (like an iceberg where ~90% is hidden underwater). 'The corruption case was just the tip of the iceberg — there were far more scandals.' Suggests there is much more below the surface.

Question 399medium

Which sentence contains a 'double negative' error?

  1. She doesn't have any money.
  2. He can't do nothing right.
  3. There is nothing wrong with the plan.
  4. She never eats junk food.
Show answer

Answer: He can't do nothing right.

Double negative: using two negative elements in one clause, which in standard English creates a positive meaning or is considered incorrect. 'He can't do nothing right' = two negatives ('can't' + 'nothing') = incorrect. Correct: 'He can't do anything right' OR 'He can do nothing right.'

Question 400hard

What is the meaning of 'hoi polloi'?

  1. The elite upper class
  2. The common people; the masses
  3. A type of food
  4. A Greek festival
Show answer

Answer: The common people; the masses

'Hoi polloi' (Greek): the masses; the common people. Often used by elites to refer to ordinary people in a somewhat dismissive way. Literally 'the many' in Greek. Note: since 'hoi' already means 'the', saying 'the hoi polloi' is technically redundant but widely accepted.

Question 401medium

What is the plural of 'index'?

  1. Indexs
  2. Indexs or Indexes
  3. Indices (formal/mathematical) or Indexes (books)
  4. Indexia
Show answer

Answer: Indices (formal/mathematical) or Indexes (books)

'Index' has two accepted plurals: 'indices' (formal, mathematical, scientific contexts) and 'indexes' (general use, especially for book indexes). 'Stock indices' or 'stock indexes' are both acceptable. Similar Latin-origin words: appendix → appendices/appendixes, vortex → vortices/vortexes.

Question 402medium

The word 'debacle' means:

  1. A great success
  2. A sudden disastrous collapse or failure; a fiasco
  3. A long debate
  4. A formal ceremony
Show answer

Answer: A sudden disastrous collapse or failure; a fiasco

Debacle: a sudden disaster or complete failure; a chaotic collapse. Synonyms: fiasco, disaster, catastrophe, shambles, rout. 'The event turned into a complete debacle when the equipment failed.' From French 'débâcle' = breaking up of ice in a river, causing sudden flooding.

Question 403medium

Which sentence correctly uses the present perfect continuous tense?

  1. She has been working on this project for three months.
  2. She was working on this project for three months.
  3. She has worked on this project since three months.
  4. She is working on this project for three months.
Show answer

Answer: She has been working on this project for three months.

Present perfect continuous: have/has + been + verb-ing. Used for actions that started in the past and are still continuing. 'She has been working for three months' (started 3 months ago, still working). Use 'for' with duration. Use 'since' with specific time: 'since March'.

Question 404hard

What is 'iambic pentameter' in poetry?

  1. A rhyme scheme with five stanzas
  2. A metrical pattern with five iambs (unstressed-stressed syllable pairs) per line, totaling 10 syllables
  3. A type of sonnet with 10 lines
  4. A free verse poem with 10 lines
Show answer

Answer: A metrical pattern with five iambs (unstressed-stressed syllable pairs) per line, totaling 10 syllables

Iambic pentameter: the most common meter in English poetry. 'Iamb' = unstressed-stressed syllable pair (da-DUM). 'Pentameter' = five feet per line. Total: 10 syllables per line. Shakespeare wrote plays and sonnets in iambic pentameter: 'Shall I | com-PARE | thee TO | a SUM | mer's DAY'.

Question 405easy

What is a 'clause' in grammar?

  1. A single word
  2. A group of words that contains a subject and a predicate (verb)
  3. A phrase without a verb
  4. A punctuation mark
Show answer

Answer: A group of words that contains a subject and a predicate (verb)

Clause: a group of words containing a subject and a verb (predicate). Two types: Independent clause (can stand alone as a sentence) and Dependent clause (cannot stand alone — needs an independent clause). A phrase lacks a subject-verb combination.

Question 406easy

What is a 'phrase' in grammar?

  1. A group of words with a subject and verb
  2. A group of words that functions as a unit but does NOT have a subject-verb combination
  3. A complete sentence
  4. A single descriptive adjective
Show answer

Answer: A group of words that functions as a unit but does NOT have a subject-verb combination

Phrase: a group of words that functions together as a unit but does NOT contain both a subject and a verb. Types: noun phrase ('the big red book'), verb phrase ('was running quickly'), prepositional phrase ('on the table'), participial phrase ('running fast'). A clause has subject + verb; a phrase does not.

Question 407medium

The expression 'carte blanche' means:

  1. A white card
  2. Complete freedom to act as one wishes; unlimited authority
  3. A blank cheque only
  4. A type of French menu
Show answer

Answer: Complete freedom to act as one wishes; unlimited authority

'Carte blanche' (French: 'blank card/document'): complete freedom to do as one wishes; unconditional authority. 'The director was given carte blanche to redesign the entire department.' Means you have permission to do whatever you think is best without restrictions.

Question 408hard

Select the sentence with the correct use of 'may have' vs 'might have':

  1. She might have left already — I'm not sure.
  2. She may have left — it's impossible.
  3. She might have left — it's certain.
  4. May have and might have cannot be used in completed action contexts.
Show answer

Answer: She might have left already — I'm not sure.

Both 'may have' and 'might have' express possibility about past events. 'She might have left already' = it's possible she has left (expresses possibility/speculation). 'Might have' often implies more doubt than 'may have'. Both are acceptable in this context, but 'might have' is the correctly used one here.

Question 409hard

The word 'inimical' means:

  1. Friendly and welcoming
  2. Hostile; tending to obstruct or harm something
  3. Familiar and comfortable
  4. Very creative and inventive
Show answer

Answer: Hostile; tending to obstruct or harm something

Inimical: tending to obstruct or harm; hostile; adverse. 'Policies inimical to free trade', 'an inimical attitude'. Synonyms: hostile, harmful, adverse, unfavorable, antagonistic. Antonyms: friendly, favorable, beneficial, conducive. From Latin 'inimicus' = enemy.

Question 410easy

What does 'to read between the lines' mean?

  1. To read very slowly and carefully
  2. To understand a hidden meaning that is not directly stated
  3. To read only alternate lines
  4. To misunderstand something completely
Show answer

Answer: To understand a hidden meaning that is not directly stated

'Read between the lines': to find a hidden meaning in something, going beyond what is literally stated. 'Reading her message carefully, he could read between the lines that she was upset.' Refers to discovering implied meaning — what isn't said directly but is suggested.

Question 411medium

'To have a chip on your shoulder' means:

  1. To carry a heavy burden
  2. To be resentful or have a grievance about something, showing it through aggressive attitude
  3. To be a skilled carpenter
  4. To have a minor injury
Show answer

Answer: To be resentful or have a grievance about something, showing it through aggressive attitude

'Have a chip on your shoulder': to act aggressively because of a persistent feeling of resentment, inferiority, or grievance (real or imagined). 'He has a chip on his shoulder about not getting the promotion.' Origin: 19th-century practice of placing a chip of wood on one's shoulder and daring others to knock it off.

Question 412easy

What is the one-word substitute for 'a person who can use both hands equally well'?

  1. Ambidextrous
  2. Ambivalent
  3. Ambiguous
  4. Amphitheater
Show answer

Answer: Ambidextrous

Ambidextrous: able to use both hands equally well. From Latin 'ambi' (both) + 'dexter' (right hand). A rare and highly useful skill. Famous ambidextrous people: Leonardo da Vinci, Benjamin Franklin. 'Ambidextrous' is also used figuratively: 'an ambidextrous politician' who can argue both sides.

Question 413easy

What is the meaning of 'nostalgia'?

  1. Fear of the future
  2. A sentimental longing or wistful affection for the past
  3. Excitement about new things
  4. Boredom with routine
Show answer

Answer: A sentimental longing or wistful affection for the past

Nostalgia: a sentimental longing for or wistful memory of the past, typically a period or place with happy personal associations. From Greek 'nostos' (return home) + 'algos' (pain). 'She felt nostalgia for her childhood summers.' The adjective is 'nostalgic': 'feeling nostalgic about the past'.

Question 414hard

Select the sentence with the correct use of 'who' in a question:

  1. Who did the teacher praise?
  2. Whom called you?
  3. Who you saw at the store?
  4. To who did she speak?
Show answer

Answer: Who did the teacher praise?

'Who did the teacher praise?' — Even though 'who' here is the object (the teacher praised someone), modern standard English often allows 'who' in questions (though 'Whom did the teacher praise?' is formally correct). 'Whom called you?' is wrong (subject position needs 'Who'). 'To who' should be 'To whom'.

Question 415medium

What is 'irony of situation' (situational irony)?

  1. Saying the opposite of what you mean
  2. When the outcome is opposite to what was expected or intended
  3. A type of sarcasm
  4. A figure of speech comparing two things
Show answer

Answer: When the outcome is opposite to what was expected or intended

Situational irony: when the actual outcome is opposite to or very different from what was expected. Examples: A fire station burning down, a police station being robbed, a marriage counselor getting divorced. Different from verbal irony (saying the opposite of what you mean) and dramatic irony (audience knows more than characters).

Question 416medium

The word 'didactic' means:

  1. Entertaining and funny
  2. Intended to teach or instruct; sometimes in a preachy way
  3. Confusing and hard to understand
  4. Very emotional
Show answer

Answer: Intended to teach or instruct; sometimes in a preachy way

Didactic: intended to teach or instruct, especially in moral or ethical lessons; sometimes implying a preachy or heavy-handed approach. 'A didactic story' explicitly teaches a lesson. Synonyms: instructive, educational, informative, preachy. Aesop's fables are classic didactic literature.

Question 417easy

What is the one-word substitute for 'a list of all the books used or referred to in a book'?

  1. Preface
  2. Glossary
  3. Bibliography
  4. Index
Show answer

Answer: Bibliography

Bibliography: a list of all works cited, referenced, or consulted in a book, article, or paper. Glossary: a list of terms with their definitions. Index: an alphabetical list of subjects/names with page numbers. Preface: an introduction written by the author. Appendix: supplementary material at the end of a book.

Question 418medium

Choose the correctly formed sentence in reported speech: He said, 'I am very tired today.'

  1. He said that he is very tired today.
  2. He said that he was very tired that day.
  3. He told that he was very tired today.
  4. He said that he had been very tired today.
Show answer

Answer: He said that he was very tired that day.

Reported speech rules: (1) Tense shifts: 'am' → 'was'; (2) Time changes: 'today' → 'that day'; (3) Pronoun changes: 'I' → 'he'. 'He said that he was very tired that day.' Note: 'tell' is used with an object ('He told me that...'); 'say' doesn't need an object. 'He told that...' is WRONG.

Question 419easy

What is the 'climax' of a story?

  1. The beginning of the story
  2. The highest point of tension or conflict in a story, after which the plot begins to resolve
  3. The resolution at the end
  4. The introduction of the main character
Show answer

Answer: The highest point of tension or conflict in a story, after which the plot begins to resolve

Climax: the moment of highest intensity or tension in the plot — the turning point where the main conflict reaches its peak. After the climax, the story enters the falling action leading to the denouement/resolution. Plot structure: Exposition → Rising Action → Climax → Falling Action → Resolution.

Question 420easy

The word 'remorse' means:

  1. Anger toward others
  2. Deep regret or guilt for wrongdoing
  3. Joy and happiness
  4. Indifference to events
Show answer

Answer: Deep regret or guilt for wrongdoing

Remorse: deep regret and guilt for a wrong committed. Synonyms: guilt, regret, contrition, penitence, shame. Antonyms: indifference, pride, shamelessness. 'He felt remorse for his harsh words.' 'Remorseful' (adjective): feeling deep regret. Remorselessly (adverb): without feeling guilty.

Question 421medium

Which sentence has the correct use of 'neither...nor'?

  1. Neither he nor she are coming to the party.
  2. Neither he nor she is coming to the party.
  3. Neither he or she is coming to the party.
  4. Neither he and she are coming to the party.
Show answer

Answer: Neither he nor she is coming to the party.

'Neither...nor' is a correlative conjunction pair. The verb agrees with the subject nearest to it: 'she' (singular) → 'is'. NEVER use 'or' with 'neither' ('neither...or' is wrong). The correct pairs are: neither...nor; either...or; both...and; not only...but also.

Question 422medium

What does 'to keep something at bay' mean?

  1. To store near the water
  2. To prevent something from getting too close or causing harm
  3. To embrace something fully
  4. To expose something to danger
Show answer

Answer: To prevent something from getting too close or causing harm

'Keep at bay': to prevent something dangerous or unpleasant from coming too close or causing harm. 'The medication helps keep the infection at bay.' 'The police kept the crowd at bay.' Origin: from hunting — when a hunted animal is cornered and faces the hounds, it is 'brought to bay'.

Question 423easy

What does 'the pen is mightier than the sword' mean?

  1. Writing materials are more expensive than weapons
  2. The power of writing and communication is more effective than military force
  3. Pens are made of stronger material than swords
  4. Intelligence is better than physical strength
Show answer

Answer: The power of writing and communication is more effective than military force

'The pen is mightier than the sword': writing (ideas, communication, knowledge) is more powerful and effective than physical force or weapons. Coined by Edward Bulwer-Lytton (1839). Also interpreted as: words/ideas can change the world more than violence. This is an example of metonymy ('pen' = writing; 'sword' = violence).

Question 424hard

What is 'pathetic fallacy' in literature?

  1. A logical error in an argument
  2. Attributing human emotions to nature or the environment to reflect a character's mood
  3. A type of comedy in drama
  4. A false statement of emotion
Show answer

Answer: Attributing human emotions to nature or the environment to reflect a character's mood

Pathetic fallacy: attributing human emotions to inanimate nature or the environment, so that the setting reflects or amplifies a character's feelings. Coined by John Ruskin. Examples: 'the angry sea', 'a weeping sky', 'the cheerful sunshine' — the nature's mood mirrors the character's. Different from personification (giving any human quality, not just emotions).

Question 425easy

What is the 'exposition' in a story's structure?

  1. The turning point or climax
  2. The background information introducing characters, setting, and initial situation
  3. The falling action after the climax
  4. The final resolution
Show answer

Answer: The background information introducing characters, setting, and initial situation

Exposition: the beginning section of a story that provides background information — introduces the main characters, setting (time and place), and the initial situation. It establishes the context before the conflict develops. Plot structure: Exposition → Rising Action → Climax → Falling Action → Resolution/Denouement.

Question 426easy

What is an 'anagram'?

  1. A word that reads the same backwards
  2. A word formed by rearranging the letters of another word
  3. A word that sounds the same as another
  4. A word with multiple meanings
Show answer

Answer: A word formed by rearranging the letters of another word

Anagram: a word or phrase formed by rearranging the letters of another word or phrase. Examples: 'listen' → 'silent', 'earth' → 'heart', 'cinema' → 'iceman', 'astronomer' → 'moon starer', 'the eyes' → 'they see'. Palindrome = reads the same backwards.

Question 427medium

The word 'ameliorate' is a synonym for:

  1. Worsen
  2. Improve
  3. Ignore
  4. Destroy
Show answer

Answer: Improve

Ameliorate: to make something bad better; to improve a situation. Synonyms: improve, better, enhance, alleviate, relieve. Antonyms: worsen, aggravate, exacerbate. 'The new policies ameliorated the condition of the workers.' From Latin 'melior' = better.

Question 428hard

Which sentence correctly uses 'imply' vs 'infer'?

  1. The author implies that the detective infers the truth.
  2. The detective implied the murderer's identity from the clues.
  3. She inferred that things would improve soon (by suggesting it).
  4. The author inferred that violence leads to destruction in his novel.
Show answer

Answer: The author implies that the detective infers the truth.

'Imply' = to suggest or indicate without stating directly (done by the speaker/writer). 'Infer' = to draw a conclusion from evidence (done by the listener/reader). 'The author implies' (the author hints/suggests). 'The detective infers' (the detective concludes from clues). Memory: Imply = suggest; Infer = deduce.

Question 429hard

The word 'dilettante' means:

  1. A highly skilled professional
  2. A person who dabbles in an art or activity without serious commitment
  3. A dedicated teacher
  4. A passionate collector
Show answer

Answer: A person who dabbles in an art or activity without serious commitment

Dilettante: a person who cultivates an interest in an art or field without real commitment or expertise; an amateur who dabbles. Often used slightly negatively. Synonyms: dabbler, amateur, trifler. Antonyms: professional, expert, dedicated practitioner. From Italian 'dilettare' = to delight.

Question 430hard

What is the correct meaning of 'status quo ante'?

  1. The current situation
  2. The state of affairs that existed before a particular event
  3. The most desirable state
  4. The official position
Show answer

Answer: The state of affairs that existed before a particular event

'Status quo ante' (Latin): the state of affairs that existed before a particular event or action. Used in law and diplomacy: 'to restore the status quo ante' = to return to the situation as it was before the conflict/event. 'Status quo' alone = the current situation.

Question 431easy

What is the meaning of the phrase 'to burn the candle at both ends'?

  1. To waste resources
  2. To work excessively hard, burning oneself out by working day and night
  3. To celebrate with candles
  4. To be very productive
Show answer

Answer: To work excessively hard, burning oneself out by working day and night

'Burn the candle at both ends': to exhaust oneself by working or socializing too hard, especially staying up very late and getting up early. 'She was burning the candle at both ends preparing for the exams while also working part-time.' Implies eventual exhaustion.

Question 432easy

In reading comprehension, 'skimming' is:

  1. Reading every word carefully
  2. Reading quickly to get the general idea of a text
  3. Reading the last paragraph only
  4. Re-reading a text multiple times
Show answer

Answer: Reading quickly to get the general idea of a text

Skimming: reading quickly over a text to get the general idea or main points — not reading every word. 'Scanning' is reading quickly to find specific information. 'Close reading' is careful, detailed reading of every word. For exam comprehension, skim first to understand the topic, then read in detail.

Question 433easy

What is a 'topic sentence'?

  1. The title of a paragraph
  2. The sentence that introduces the main idea of a paragraph, usually at the beginning
  3. The last sentence that concludes a paragraph
  4. A sentence with an important vocabulary word
Show answer

Answer: The sentence that introduces the main idea of a paragraph, usually at the beginning

Topic sentence: the sentence (usually the first) in a paragraph that states the main idea or point of that paragraph. All other sentences in the paragraph support, explain, or develop the topic sentence. Good paragraphs have one clear topic sentence.

Question 434medium

  2. Bitterness or ill feeling in speech or manner
  3. Enthusiasm and energy
  4. Sadness and depression

Answer: Bitterness or ill feeling in speech or manner

Acrimony: bitterness or ill-feeling expressed in speech or behaviour. Synonyms: bitterness, ill will, resentment, hostility, rancour. Antonyms: goodwill, friendliness, goodness, amity. 'The divorce proceedings were conducted with great acrimony.' Adjective: acrimonious.

Question 435easy

What is the one-word substitute for 'one who can neither read nor write'?

  1. Ignorant
  2. Illiterate
  3. Uneducated
  4. Aliterate
Show answer

Answer: Illiterate

Illiterate: unable to read or write. Literate = able to read and write. Functionally illiterate = cannot read/write well enough for daily needs. Semi-literate = can read and write to a limited degree. Aliterate = able to read but choosing not to. UNESCO considers literacy a basic human right.

'Prolix' means tediously long and wordy — a synonym of 'verbose'. Both mean using more words than necessary. Other synonyms: garrulous, long-winded, loquacious, rambling, wordy. Antonyms: concise, succinct, terse, laconic, brief.

Question 437easy

  3. To revive an old project
  4. To be very aggressive

Answer: To waste effort on something that cannot be changed or is already settled

'Beat a dead horse': to waste time and effort trying to change something that is already settled, or to continue discussing a matter that has already been decided. 'Stop arguing about it — you're beating a dead horse.' Synonyms: waste effort, belabour the point.

Question 438easy

What is the meaning of the idiom 'to have the last laugh'?

  1. To tell the funniest joke
  2. To ultimately succeed after being doubted or ridiculed
  3. To laugh at inappropriate times
  4. To win a comedy competition

Answer: To ultimately succeed after being doubted or ridiculed

'Have the last laugh': to ultimately be proven right or to succeed, especially after being doubted, criticized, or mocked. 'They laughed at his business idea, but he had the last laugh when it became a huge success.' Similar: 'He who laughs last, laughs longest.'

Question 439medium

Which is the correct indirect speech for: She asked, 'Are you feeling well?'

  1. She asked if I was feeling well.
  2. She asked that if I am feeling well.
  3. She asked whether I am feeling well.
  4. She asked that I am feeling well.
Show answer

Answer: She asked if I was feeling well.

Indirect speech for yes/no questions uses 'if' or 'whether': 'She asked if/whether I was feeling well.' Tense shift: 'are' → 'was'. Pronoun change: 'you' → 'I'. Do NOT use 'that' for questions. No question mark in indirect speech.

Question 440hard

The word 'pejorative' means:

  1. Very high praise
  2. Expressing disapproval or contempt; having a negative connotation
  3. Completely neutral
  4. Expressing enthusiasm
Show answer

Answer: Expressing disapproval or contempt; having a negative connotation

Pejorative: a word or phrase expressing contempt or disapproval. 'Bureaucrat' can be used pejoratively to imply inefficiency. 'Slang' can be pejorative. Also used as an adjective: 'a pejorative term'. Synonyms: derogatory, disparaging, negative. Antonym: complimentary, laudatory.

Question 441hard

Identify the sentence with correct subject-verb agreement:

  1. Bread and butter are his favourite breakfast.
  2. Bread and butter is his favourite breakfast.
  3. Bread and butter were his favourite breakfast.
  4. Bread and butter have been his favourite breakfast.
Show answer

Answer: Bread and butter is his favourite breakfast.

When two nouns joined by 'and' refer to a single concept or item (always eaten/used together), they take a singular verb: 'bread and butter is his favourite.' Similarly: 'fish and chips is', 'law and order is'. When they refer to separate items: 'bread and milk are on the table' (two separate things).

Question 442medium

  2. Calm and relaxed; showing a casual lack of concern
  3. Anxious and nervous
  4. Very angry

Answer: Calm and relaxed; showing a casual lack of concern

Nonchalant: feeling or appearing casually calm and relaxed; showing a lack of concern. 'She acted nonchalant about the test results, though she was nervous inside.' Synonyms: casual, cool, laid-back, indifferent, unconcerned. Antonyms: anxious, concerned, worried, agitated.

Question 443easy

Carpenter: a person who makes and repairs wooden objects and structures, including furniture. Mason: works with stone/brick. Potter: makes pottery/ceramic items. Cobbler: makes and repairs shoes. Tailor: makes clothes. Weaver: makes fabric. Blacksmith: works with iron/metal.

Question 444medium

What is the meaning of the phrase 'every cloud has a silver lining' — which literary device is this?

  1. Simile
  2. Metaphor (silver lining = something positive in something dark/difficult)
  3. Hyperbole
  4. Personification
Show answer

Answer: Metaphor (silver lining = something positive in something dark/difficult)

'Every cloud has a silver lining' is a metaphor. A 'cloud' metaphorically represents a difficult or dark situation; a 'silver lining' metaphorically represents something positive within it. The proverb uses metaphorical language to convey the idea that every difficult situation has some positive aspect.

Question 445easy

What does 'to cost an arm and a leg' mean?

  2. To be extremely expensive
  3. To require great physical effort
  4. To take a very long time

Answer: To be extremely expensive

'Cost an arm and a leg': to be extremely expensive. 'That new car cost him an arm and a leg.' Synonyms: cost a fortune, be very expensive, cost the earth. Origin: possibly from 18th/19th century portrait painters who charged more for full-length portraits including arms and legs.

Question 446hard

The word 'soporific' means:

  1. Energizing and stimulating
  2. Tending to induce sleep; causing drowsiness
  3. Very exciting
  4. Refreshing and invigorating
Show answer

Answer: Tending to induce sleep; causing drowsiness

Soporific: tending to induce sleep; drowsy. 'The soporific effect of the lecture was evident from the nodding heads.' Synonyms: sleep-inducing, drowsy, sedative, hypnotic. Antonyms: stimulating, invigorating, energizing. A 'soporific drug' = a sleeping pill.

Question 447medium

Which sentence uses the 'passive voice' in the present perfect tense?

  1. She has written a letter.
  2. A letter has been written by her.
  3. She wrote a letter.
  4. A letter was written by her.
Show answer

Answer: A letter has been written by her.

Present perfect passive: have/has + been + past participle. 'A letter has been written by her.' Present perfect active: 'She has written a letter.' Past simple passive: 'A letter was written.' Past perfect passive: 'A letter had been written.' Present simple passive: 'A letter is written.'

Question 448hard

What is an 'antithesis' as a rhetorical device?

  1. A figure of speech comparing two unlike things
  2. The juxtaposition of contrasting ideas in balanced phrases or sentences
  3. Repetition of the same words at the start of clauses
  4. An indirect expression replacing an unpleasant term
Show answer

Answer: The juxtaposition of contrasting ideas in balanced phrases or sentences

Antithesis: placing contrasting ideas in parallel structure for emphasis. Famous examples: 'To be or not to be' (Shakespeare); 'It was the best of times, it was the worst of times' (Dickens); 'Give every man thy ear, but few thy voice' (Shakespeare). Creates a strong contrast through parallel structure.

Question 449medium

What does 'quid pro quo' mean?

  1. Something for nothing
  2. A favour or advantage given in return for something of equal value; an exchange
  3. An unfair advantage
  4. A one-sided agreement
Show answer

Answer: A favour or advantage given in return for something of equal value; an exchange

'Quid pro quo' (Latin: 'something for something'): a favour or advantage given in return for something of equal value; a mutual exchange. 'The deal was a quid pro quo — we'd provide the land, they'd build the facility.' Common in business, law, and politics.

Question 450medium

Which of the following sentences uses an adverbial clause of purpose?

  1. He ran so that he would not miss the bus.
  2. He ran because he was late.
  3. He ran when the bell rang.
  4. Although he was tired, he ran.
Show answer

Answer: He ran so that he would not miss the bus.

Adverbial clause of purpose: answers 'why' or 'for what purpose'. Introduced by 'so that', 'in order that', 'lest'. 'He ran SO THAT he would not miss the bus' — the purpose of running was to not miss the bus. 'Because' = cause. 'When' = time. 'Although' = concession.

Question 451hard

Choose the sentence with the correctly used 'either...or':

  1. Either she or her friends was responsible.
  2. Either she or her friends were responsible.
  3. Either she or her friends is responsible.
  4. Either she or friends were responsible.
Show answer

Answer: Either she or her friends were responsible.

With 'either...or', the verb agrees with the subject nearest to it (proximity rule). 'Either she or her friends were responsible' — 'friends' (plural) is nearest to the verb → 'were'. If reversed: 'Either her friends or she was responsible' — 'she' (singular) nearest → 'was'.

Question 452easy

What does the phrase 'let the cat out of the bag' mean?

  1. To release a cat
  2. To accidentally reveal a secret
  3. To create confusion
  4. To lose something valuable
Show answer

Answer: To accidentally reveal a secret

'Let the cat out of the bag': to accidentally or carelessly reveal a secret. 'I was planning a surprise party but she let the cat out of the bag.' Origin possibly from marketplace fraud — selling a cat (less valuable) hidden in a bag instead of a piglet. Synonyms: spill the beans, reveal the secret, give the game away.

Question 453medium

What is an 'epithet' in literary terms?

  1. A tombstone inscription
  2. An adjective or phrase expressing a quality or attribute of the person/thing mentioned
  3. A short poem at the start of a book
  4. A witty saying
Show answer

Answer: An adjective or phrase expressing a quality or attribute of the person/thing mentioned

Epithet: an adjective or phrase used to characterize a person or thing, expressing their quality or attribute. Examples: 'Alexander the Great', 'Honest Abe' (Lincoln), 'Peter the Great', 'Richard the Lionheart'. Also: 'the rosy-fingered dawn' (Homer). Sometimes used pejoratively as an insult.

Question 454medium

What is the meaning of 'lingua franca'?

  1. A beautiful language
  2. A common language used between speakers of different native languages
  3. The French language
  4. An extinct language
Show answer

Answer: A common language used between speakers of different native languages

Lingua franca: a language that is adopted as a common means of communication between speakers whose native languages are different. English serves as the global lingua franca in business, science, and diplomacy. Historical examples: Latin (medieval Europe), Swahili (East Africa).

Question 455medium

What does 'to have a method to one's madness' mean?

  1. To be genuinely insane
  2. To have a logical or sensible plan behind apparently crazy behaviour
  3. To be unpredictable
  4. To follow unusual procedures
Show answer

Answer: To have a logical or sensible plan behind apparently crazy behaviour

'Method in one's madness': a sensible or logical reason behind seemingly strange or chaotic behaviour. 'His approach seemed chaotic, but there was method in his madness — he was testing everyone.' From Shakespeare's Hamlet: 'Though this be madness, yet there is method in't.'

Question 456easy

The phrase 'by and large' means:

  1. In great detail
  2. On the whole; generally speaking
  3. In small amounts
  4. Specifically and precisely
Show answer

Answer: On the whole; generally speaking

'By and large': on the whole; generally speaking; mostly. 'By and large, the students performed well in the exam.' Synonyms: generally, mostly, on the whole, in general. Origin: nautical — a ship sailing 'by and large' was sailing in a variety of wind directions.

Question 457hard

What is the literary device 'bathos'?

  1. The highest point of excitement in a narrative
  2. An abrupt, disappointing transition from the elevated to the trivial or ridiculous
  3. A type of understatement
  4. A comparison using 'like'
Show answer

Answer: An abrupt, disappointing transition from the elevated to the trivial or ridiculous

Bathos: an abrupt, jarring shift from the serious, elevated, or sublime to the trivial, ridiculous, or mundane, producing an anticlimatic effect. 'For God, for Country, and for the cricket team.' Can be deliberate (for comic effect) or unintentional (a writing flaw). Coined by Alexander Pope.

Question 458medium

What is the correct use of a 'colon' in a sentence?

  1. To join two independent clauses of equal weight
  2. To introduce a list, explanation, or quotation after a complete clause
  3. Before a coordinating conjunction
  4. After an opening salutation in informal letters
Show answer

Answer: To introduce a list, explanation, or quotation after a complete clause

Colon: used after a complete independent clause to introduce a list, explanation, definition, or quotation. 'She had three options: leave, stay, or negotiate.' 'He had one goal: to win.' The clause before a colon must be complete (could stand alone). Don't use a colon after a verb or preposition directly.

Question 459hard

What is the difference between 'that' and 'which' in relative clauses?

  1. They are completely interchangeable
  2. 'That' is used for defining (restrictive) clauses; 'which' is used for non-defining (non-restrictive) clauses preceded by a comma
  3. 'Which' is used for people; 'that' for animals
  4. 'That' is informal; 'which' is formal
Show answer

Answer: 'That' is used for defining (restrictive) clauses; 'which' is used for non-defining (non-restrictive) clauses preceded by a comma

'That' introduces restrictive (defining) relative clauses (no comma): 'The book that I borrowed was fascinating' (identifies which book). 'Which' introduces non-restrictive (non-defining) clauses (with commas): 'The book, which I borrowed from the library, was fascinating' (adds extra info about an already-identified book).

Question 460medium

What is the meaning of 'serendipity'?

  1. A planned, deliberate discovery
  2. The occurrence of events by chance in a happy or beneficial way
  3. A very unfortunate accident
  4. A careful and methodical search
Show answer

Answer: The occurrence of events by chance in a happy or beneficial way

Serendipity: the occurrence and development of events by chance in a happy or beneficial way; finding something good without looking for it. 'The discovery of penicillin was a serendipitous accident.' Coined by Horace Walpole (1754) from the Persian fairy tale 'The Three Princes of Serendip' (ancient name of Sri Lanka).

Question 461hard

Select the sentence where 'that' introduces a noun clause:

  1. The book that I borrowed is overdue.
  2. She said that she would come tomorrow.
  3. The car that he bought is red.
  4. The place that she visited was beautiful.
Show answer

Answer: She said that she would come tomorrow.

'That' can introduce: (1) a noun clause (acts as subject/object): 'She said THAT she would come' — the 'that' clause is the object of 'said'. (2) a relative (adjective) clause: 'the book THAT I borrowed' — modifies 'book'. In option B, 'that she would come' = noun clause (object of 'said').

  1. Economic growth is fundamentally incompatible with ecological preservation.
  2. Current economic models are flawed because they fail to integrate the true cost of environmental degradation.
  3. The biosphere's thresholds are unknown, making it impossible to determine the limits of economic growth.
  4. Sustainability can be achieved by strictly regulating the industries that cause the most pollution.
Show answer

Answer: Current economic models are flawed because they fail to integrate the true cost of environmental degradation.

The passage highlights the 'fallacy' of treating natural capital as a subsidy and the need to stop 'externalizing' costs.

Question 764hard

However, an obsession with total visibility can inadvertently incentivize 'performative compliance,' where officials prioritize the appearance of adherence to rules over the actual achievement of substantive outcomes. When the metric of success shifts from the quality of the result to the transparency of the process, the bureaucratic machinery may become more rigid, stifling the discretionary flexibility necessary for innovative problem-solving in complex societal crises. Which of the following is the most rational inference that can be drawn from the passage?

  1. Transparency is an ineffective tool for curbing corruption in modern governance.
  2. The drive for total visibility can paradoxically undermine the efficacy of governance by prioritizing form over substance.
  3. Discretionary flexibility in bureaucracy is inherently contradictory to the principles of transparent governance.
  4. Performative compliance is a necessary evil to ensure that bureaucratic processes remain accountable to the public.
Show answer

Answer: The drive for total visibility can paradoxically undermine the efficacy of governance by prioritizing form over substance.

The passage argues that an obsession with transparency can lead to performative compliance, shifting focus from results to process and thus undermining actual efficacy.
